REPUBLIC OF TURKEY
SECOND PROGRAMMATIC FINANCIAL AND PUBLIC SECTOR ADJUSTMENT LOAN
LOAN SUNIMARY
Borrower:                   The Republic of Tirjcey
Amount:                    US$1.35 billion
Terrns:                    US$550 million on standard IBRD terms, payable in 15 years
including 5 years of grace and level principal repayment, at six
months LIBOR for US Dollars plus variable spread for
Variable Single Currency Loans; and US$800 million on
special structural adjustment loan (SSAL) terms, payable in 5
years including 3 years of grace and level principal repayment,
"at six months LIBOR for US Dollar plus 400 basis points.
Front End Fee:              I percent of Loan amounts
Commitment Fee:            0.75 percent on undisbursed loan balances, beginning 60 days
after signing, less any waiver on the US$550 million amount
on standard IBRD terms.
Objectives and Description:  The proposed Second Programmatic Financial and Public
Sector Adjustment Loan (PFPSAL II) is the second Loan in
support of the Governnent's multi-year financial and public
sector reform program. It follows on the first Programmatic
Financial and Public Sector Adjustment Loan of US$ 1.1 billion
approved in July 2001 (US$700 million on standard I3RD
terms and US$400 million on SSAL terms). The main
objective of the proposed PFPSAL 11 is to support the next
phase of the Government's financial and public sector reform
priorities in response to the February 2001 financial crisis,
while continuing to ensure that social programs are adequately
funded. Key priorities include, in the financial sector: (i)
overhaul of the regulatory framework for banking activity, (ii)
institutional development of the new Bank Regulation and
Supervision Agency (BRSA), (iii) problem bank/bank failure
resolution, and (iv) state bank restructuring and privatization;
and in the public sector: (a) a further deepening of structural
fiscal policies in support of sustainable fiscal adjustment, (b) a
broadening  of  the  Governnent's  public  expenditure
management reform program including the implementation of
concrete improvements to the framework for budget planning
and execution, financial accountability, and public liability
This document has a restricted distribution and may be used by recipients only in the
performance of their official duties. Its contents may not otherwise be disclosed without
World Bank authorization.



management, and (c) implementation of the Government's
strategy to strengthen public sector governance including
adoption and implementation of a national anti-corruption
strategy and initial steps towards civil service reform.
Benefits:                   The principal benefits of the Loan will be to: (i) support the
Government's macroeconomic policy framework which aims
to overcome the affects of the February crisis and create the
conditions for renewed growth and disinflation, (ii) restore
confidence in the banking system; (iii) strengthen the
foundation for an efficient and sound banking system which
can be competitive in quality and performance at the
international level; (iv) reduce the vulnerability of the banking
system and enhance its capacity to withstand external shocks
and thereby reduce systemic failure risk; (v) position Turkey's
banking sector for accession to the European Union (EU) by
starting the process of aligning the prudential regime for the
banking system with applicable EU banking sector directives;
(vi) support structural fiscal measures to underpin the
sustainability and quality of the fiscal adjustment needed to
achieve macroeconomic stability while ensuring that social
spending is protected; (vii) lay the foundation for durable
improvements to public expenditure management and fiscal
transparency in line with international standards; and (viii)
raise the quality of public sector governance. The PFPSAL II
will provide essential budgetary support to help the
Government finance the costs of recovering from the crisis
while continuing to fund its critical social programs.
Risks:                      The risks associated with the Government's financial and
public sector reform  program  arise from  macroeconomic
factors, political considerations and institutional weaknesses.
The key macroeconomic risk is that real interest rates will
remain higher than projected which would delay the economic
recovery and affect public debt sustainability. A related
macroeconomic risk factor is Turkey's vulnerability to both
internal and external shocks as evidenced by the impact of the
September 11 events. The commercialization and privatization
of the state-owned banks is a major political and organizational
challenge for the Govermnent. The rapid restructuring of Halk
and Ziraat also has significant fiscal and social implications.
While the Government has so far been able to raise sufficient
resources to finance the restructuring of the state banks and the
resolution of the banks under the Savings Deposit Insurance
Fund (SDIF), the public debt has increased sharply as a share
of GNP. Continued tight fiscal discipline and further reform



actions to bolster the confidence of domestic and international
investors in Turkish sovereign debt instruments will be
essential. Finally, satisfactory and timely failure resolution
efforts by the SDIF (for already intervened banks) and
enforcement of capital restoration plans by the BRSA (for non-
intervened banks) are important; the risk that such efforts will
fall short of what is needed is related to the political pressure
that may be put on these agencies for a slowdown of regulatory
enforcement action and general regulatory forbearance. The
public sector reform agenda is politically sensitive and may be
jeopardized by resistance of vested interest groups that would
prefer to maintain the status quo. Capacity constraints and
crisis management efforts at key Government agencies may
also slow down the pace of the envisaged public sector
reforms.
Schedule of Disbursements:  US$450 million (US$184 million on standard IBRD terms and
US$266 million on SSAL terms), including a 1 percent fee to
be paid to the IBRD, immediately after loan effectiveness
expected in April 2002, a second tranche of US$450 million
(US$183 million on standard IBRD terms and US$267 million
on SSAL terms) after fulfillment of second tranche release
conditions, and a third tranche of US$450 million (US$183
million on standard IIBRD terms and US$267 million on SSAL
terms) after fulfillment of third tranche release conditions.

REPORT AND RECOMMENDATION OF THE PRESIDENT OF THE
INTERNATIONAL BANK FOR RECONSTRUCTION AND DEVELOPMENT
TO THE EXECUTIVE DIRECTORS ON A PROPOSED
SECOND PROGRAMMATIC FINANCIAL AND PUBLIC SECTOR
ADJUSTMENT LOAN
TO THE REPUBLIC OF TURKEY
1.    I submit for your approval the following Report and Recommendation on a proposed
second Programmatic Financial and Public Sector Adjustment Loan (PFPSAL II) to the Republic
of Turkey for US$1.35 billion to continue the Bank's support for the Government's multi-year
financial and public sector program. This program aims to restore confidence in the banking
system and correct the underlying structural problems in the public sector that created the
conditions under which financial crises like those which hit Turkey in November 2000 and
February 2001 could occur. The proposed loan will be in US Dollar Single Currency, partly on
standard IBRD terms (US$550 million) and partly on special structural adjustment loan terms
(US$800 million). The loan will be disbursed in three tranches. The first US$450 million
tranche (US$184 million on standard IBRD terms and US$266 million on SSAL terms) will be
disbursed immediately upon effectiveness, and the second and third tranches of US$450 million
each (US$183 million on standard IBRD terms and US$267 million on SSAL terms) upon
fulfillment of the second and third tranche release conditions as long as overall progress with the
program is also satisfactory.
2.    The proposed PFPSAL II is the second in a series of programmatic loans to be
implemented over the 2001-03 period in support of the Government's comprehensive reform of
the financial and public sectors. Under the PFPSAL program, the World Bank intends to
disburse a total of US$2.45 billion in adjustment lending to meet Turkey's external and
budgetary financing requirements arising from the crisis, and to support its short and medium
term reform goals in the financial and public sectors. The PFPSAL II follows on the first
Programmatic Financial and Public Sector Adjustment Loan (PFPSAL) of US$1.1 billion
(US$700 million on standard IBRD terms and US$400 million on SSAL terms) disbursed in a
single tranche in July 2001. Bank support for sustained implementation of reform in the
financial and public sectors would continue in the high case lending scenario through a series of
further programmatic loans as described in the CAS Progress Report (Report No. 22282-TU)
discussed by the Board together with the first PFPSAL in July 2001. Specifically, two
Programmatic Financial Sector Adjustment Loans (PFSAL I and II) of US$500 million each and
a Programmatic Public Sector Adjustment Loan (PPSAL) of US$375 million are envisaged. A
second PPSAL is tentatively envisaged beyond the current CAS period.
I.    INTRODUCTION
A.    Recent Economic Developments
3.    Macroeconomic outcomes have been mixed since the first PFPSAL was approved in
July. The program put in place in May 2001 in response to the currency crisis of February 2001
was designed to help Turkey rebound from the February shock and quickly resume economic

growth and disinflation. It combined tight demand management-centered on a large primary
surplus-with a market-based approach to managing the growing public debt burden, accelerated
financial sector restructuring and a large inflow of external official financing. However,
outcomes deviated significantly from program targets and Turkey was hit by another external
shock following the events of September 11. The financial turmoil which followed the decision
in February to abandon the crawling peg exchange rate and float the Lira has been contained, but
this relative stability remains fragile. While the price spike following the initial devaluation in
February was fairly quickly absorbed, inflationary pressures remain significant. Interest rates
were brought down from crisis peaks, but remained above the original May program path
through the end of 2001, due in part to the higher-than-expected inflation. After falling below
1.6 million TL/USD in October 2001, the exchange rate stabilized by the end of the year, albeit
at a more depreciated level than originally projected. The external current account improved
sharply registering a surplus of about US$3.3 billion (2.2 percent of GNP) compared to the
deficit of US$7.6 billion (4.9 percent of GNP) in 2000 (Table 1). Preliminary data indicate that
the fiscal program met the 5.5 percent of GNP primary surplus target for the consolidated public
sector for 2001, but the fiscal cost of bank restructuring contributed to a sharp increase in the
public debt.



-3 -
4.     The most immediate problem is that the economic recession has been much deeper than
projected and recovery has been delayed. A serious drought exacerbated the recession with
agricultural output contracting by some 3.2 percent through the first nine months of 2001. Real
credit to the economy contracted by about 30 percent during the year as banks cut back on
lending and enterprises reduced borrowing in the face of high real interest rates. Output
contracted by 8.5 percent in the 3rd quarter of 2001, compared to 11.4 percent in the 2nd quarter,
bringing the fall for the first nine months of the year to 8.3 percent. The combination of high
interest rates, large devaluation and deep recession caused the stock of public debt as a share of
GNP to rise significantly more than projected under the May program. This occurred despite the
disbursement of US$10.9 billion in combined IMF support (US$9.8 billion) and World Bank
adjustment lending (US$ 1.1 billion) between May and December. The ratio of public debt (net
of the net assets of the central bank) to GNP is now estimated to have increased from 57.4
percent of GNP at the end of 2000 to about 91 percent by the end of 2001, 12 percentage points
higher than originally projected in May.
5.     The costs of cleaning up the banking sector has added over US$40 billion to the
Treasury's debt in 2001, although about half of this represents losses on the books of the state
and SDIF banks before the crisis which were already included in the broader definition of the
public debt. The stock of Treasury securities issued for bank restructuring (including revaluation
of FX denominated securities) is estimated to have reached TL 60 quadrillion (33 percent of
GNP) by the end of 2001 (Table 2). While the Treasury has restructured recapitalization
securities held by the central bank and the state banks in order to lengthen maturities and reduce
interest payments, the risks to public debt sustainability will remain significant through the
medium term.

6.    When positive signs began to emerge by late summer, including the raising of new
syndicated loans by several large local banks, Turkey was suddenly hit hard as financial markets
reacted adversely to the terrorist attack in the United States. In the days following September 11,
secondary market rates on government securities increased by nearly 1,500 basis points, the
Istanbul Stock Exchange fell almost 20 percent and the exchange rate depreciated by a further 10
percent. This reflected in part the global impact on emerging markets, but it also highlighted
Turkey's macroeconomic vulnerability and perceived status as a frontline state in the struggle
against terrorism. The external shock arising from September 11 is expected to have a
continuing impact on Turkey's internal and external balances through numerous channels
including lower exports arising from the slowdown in Europe and lost tourism revenues. The
IMF has estimated that the impact of the shock on the balance of payrnents is in the range of
US$10 billion in 2002 and that Turkey faced a budget financing gap on the order of US$7 billion
in 2002 prior to the agreement on a new Stand-by Arrangement. Although Turkey's financial
markets quickly regained the ground lost in the immediate aftermath of September 11, this was
mainly due to investor expectations that the country would benefit from additional support from
official creditors to help overcome the negative effects of the shock.
B.    Turkey's Medium-term Macroeconomic Framework
7.     In December 2001, Turkey adopted a new medium-term macroeconomic framework in
agreement with the IMF and a renewed program   of structural reforms worked out in
collaboration with the Bank. The renewed program incorporates the three-pronged strategy
articulated by the Government last May: (i) macroeconomic policies Reared towards restoring
financial stability and growth, and resuming the disinflation process; (ii) structural policies
aimed at correctin,g the financial sector and public sector weaknesses underlving the crisis and
establishing a more sound basis for disinflation and growth; and (iii) strong social policies
including enhanced social dialogue to achieve price and wage policies consistent with
macroeconomic stability, and increased emphasis on the protection of the most vulnerable
groups of society.
8.    The renewed program aims to restore economic growth, albeit more gradually than
originally envisaged under the May program. Growth of three percent is projected in 2002,
rising to five percent in 2003-04 (Table 1). Exports and a return of private demand for
consumption and investment are expected to lead the recovery. The program targets a reduction
in inflation to 35 percent by the end of the year through tight monetary policies backed by the
introduction of inflation targeting during the course of 2002 if pre-conditions are met. Inflation
is projected to fall further to 20 percent by the end of 2003 and 12 percent in 2004. The program
aims to ensure sustainability of the public debt through stronger fiscal adjustment and additional
external financing for the budget. The primary surplus of the consolidated public sector is
targeted to increase to 6.5 percent of GNP in 2002 and to remain at this level in 2003-04. The
provision of additional external financing for the budget in 2002 is expected to reduce the
amount of government domestic borrowing and ease the pressure on domestic banks to roll over
their maturing government securities. The stock of net public sector debt is projected to fall to
81 percent of GNP in 2002 and to 69 percent of GNP by the end of 2004. Finally, the program
targets a rebuilding of international reserves to US$24 billion by 2003, slightly above the level
reached in 2000, through the provision of additional medium-term financing from the IMF



- 5 -
together with a recovery of private capital inflows beginning in 2003. Reserves would increase
further in 2004 under the assumption that confidence will continue to improve.
9.    Support for the program from the international community remains strong. The IMF's
Board of Directors approved on February 4, 2002 a new SDR 12.8 billion (US$16.2 billion)
Stand-by Arrangement for Turkey covering the 2002-04 period. This amount includes US$4.2
billion in undisbursed fumds from the previous Stand-by program in addition to US$12 billion in
new financing. This brings total IMF commitmnents for the program to over US$30 billion since
the original Stand-by was approved in December 1999. The new program has been prepared in
close cooperation with the Bank. The prior actions, performance criteria and structural
benchmarks for the new Stand-by are fully consistent with the Bank's Economic Reform Loan
(ERL) and the PFPSAL program. The World Bank has disbursed US$1.9 billion in adjustment
lending since 2000 and an additional US$3.4 billion is available under the current CAS high
case--including the proposed PFPSAL II, second tranche of the Economic Reform Loan (ERL),
and the adjustment components of the Agriculture Reform Implementation Project (ARIP) and
Social Risk Mitigation Project (SRMP)--assuming satisfactory outcomes for both
macroeconomic stabilization and structural reform. Total IFI financing for the budget deficit in
2002 is projected to reach up to US$10 billion of which US$7 billion would come from the IMF
and up to US$3 billion from the World Bank including disbursement of investment lending. The
external financing plan underpinning the new medium-term macroeconomic framework is
summarized in Table 14 in the risk section below.
10.   Markets have reacted favorably to the renewed program and the announcement of
additional external support. There are some indications that the recession may have bottomed
out. Capacity utilization was 72.3 percent in February 2002 up from 70.9 percent one year ago,
but below January's 74.1 percent. Interest rates on short-term bonds have fallen below 70
percent, in parallel with reductions in overnight rates set by the CBT, and are on a declining path
in line with the new macro-framework. The exchange rate has stabilized in the range of 1.3-1.4
million TL/USD. There are indications of fresh private capital inflows in the stock exchange,
which has rebounded strongly by some 50 percent in Lira terms since mid-September, and local
banks have been increasingly successful in rolling over their syndicated loans. Some Turkish
corporations that have succeeded in restructuring their operations have seen their access to
external capital restored. Spreads on USD denominated Turkish eurobonds have fallen by more
than 300 basis points since October. The Treasury successfully issued EURO 800 million and
US$750 million in new securities on international markets in October-December 2001,
exceeding expectations, followed by an additional US$1.45 billion in January-March 2002.
11.   The first IMF program review under the new Stand-by arrangement is expected to be
completed in April 2001. An IMF review mission visited Turkey in March and all of the
quantitative targets for the review are expected to have been met including performance criteria
for the fiscal and monetary programs. According to the mission, the prospects for meeting the 35
percent end-year inflation target have improved. The public sector debt is on track to reach or
fall below the program projection for end-2002 of 81 percent. Of some concern, is the rapid
appreciation of the real exchange rate which inay affect export performance in 2002. However,
the mission found that the external current account remains broadly on track with a better than
expected outturn for 2001 offsetting the impact of unanticipated real exchange rate appreciation



- 6 -
and higher oil prices. Recent improvements in Turkey's access to international markets could
lead to a modest increase in gross intemational reserves above the US$22 billion end-2002 target
(Table 1).
12.   The improvements in Turkey's macro-financial indicators in recent months remain
tentative, and represent in part the expected rebound from the overshooting seen in other crisis
countries. Sustained improvements in market conditions and economic recovery in the real
sector will depend on continued progress in implementing the program.  Determined
implementation of the financial and public sector reforms supported by the PFPSAL II and
subsequent programmatic operations will be critical to this effort.
C.    The Structural Reform Agenda
13.   The Government's program continues to emphasize the critical role of structural reforms
in overcoming the crisis and creating the foundations for renewed growth and financial stability.
Since the first PFPSAL was approved in July 2001, the Government has made significant
progress in implementing its agenda to restructure the foundations of the Turkish economy. The
first reform priority has been a major acceleration of financial sector reform. The financial
sector reform program encompasses continued improvements to the prudential and institutional
framework for bank supervision in line with Basel and EU standards. With regard to bank
restructuring, with the exception of two banks whose resolution has been halted by the courts, all
banks intervened by the SDIF before November 2001 were resolved by end-January 2002,
meeting a crucial program benchmark. In addition, the programn focuses on the restructuring and
privatization of the state banks.  The second core direction for structural reform is the
comprehensive reform of the public sector to address the underlying structural causes of
Turkey's macroeconomic instability, ensure medium-term fiscal sustainability, and increase the
transparency and efficiency of public expenditure management. The public sector reform
program includes structural fiscal reforms, institutional and policy reforms to improve public
expenditure management, and action to strengthen public sector governance and fight corruption.
In response to the sharp increase in public indebtedness in 2001, the Government has decided to
introduce more ambitious structural fiscal reforms starting in 2002. An initial set of measures
has been taken in the fiscal package for 2002 to streamline the public sector in line with medium-
term resource constraints and the long-term objective of promoting private sector development
and employment creation. The third direction is continuation of vital reforms in the agriculture,
energy and telecommunications sectors to improve the climate for private investment in an effort
to raise productivity, growth and incomes. Many of the latter reforms also have a structural
fiscal dimension in that they allow the state to downsize the public sector, modernize agriculture
support policies and reduce the government's contingent liabilities.
14.   Turkey needs comprehensive financial and public sector reforms to break definitively
with its troubled history of inadequate transparency, public deficits, inflation and financial
instability. A quick retrospective highlights the structural weaknesses in both the public and
financial sectors which contributed to macroeconomic instability and created the pre-conditions
for the February 2001 crisis. Chronic public sector deficits led to the buildup of debt which
financed rapid expansion of the banking sector which greatly outpaced the development of the
regulatory framework.  Macroeconomic distortions arising from fiscal imbalance created



- 7 -
incentives for the banks to take on excessive risk which was not counterbalanced by strong,
transparent regulation. The absence of comprehensive, accurate and timely fiscal information-
particularly the reliance on off-budget funds and quasi-fiscal obligations including the so-called
"duty losses" of the state banks--reinforced political and institutional obstacles to structural
change and sustainable fiscal adjustment. Unhealthy interactions between inadequate fiscal and
financial transparency compounded the risks to the economy and helped ensure the buildup of a
major systemic crisis following earlier cycles of crisis and aborted stabilization efforts
throughout the 1990s. The Government is now working to correct the underlying structural
weaknesses in the financial sector, while undertaking urgent financial restructuring to address the
impact of the crisis on the banks. In parallel, institutional and policy reforms are being initiated
to address systemic problems in the public sector, together with stronger structural measures to
underpin the fiscal adjustment. These reforms will complement on-going actions to improve the
climate for private investment and promote growth. Restoring investor confidence and economic
growth depends critically on the Government's ability to simultaneously address the structural
roots of the economic crisis and finance the costs of cleaning up the banking sector.
D.    Social and Poverty Impact of the Crisis and Reform Program
15.   Further assessment work since July 2001 tends to confirrn the heavy social impact of the
crisis and growing risk of poverty and vulnerability. Analysis, including the Corporate Sector
Assessment updated through the first three quarters of 2001, indicates that:
* Industrial production and private sector capacity utilization remain some 10 percentage
points below the levels of 2000.
*  Survey results by the Istanbul Chamber of Industry for the first half of 2001 based on a
sample of a total of 934 small, medium and large enterprises show that the rate of job
loss doubled compared to 2000, reaching 7.7 percent with small and medium-scale
enterprises reporting the highest rate of attrition.
*  Latest data from SIS show that the official unemployment rate was 10.6 percent in the
fourth quarter of 2001, compared to 6.3 percent one year ago; however, for educated
youth, the unemployment rate increased to 27.0 percent, compared to 22.7 percent in
2000(Q4).
16.   The Government is carrying out extensive survey work at the household level with
support from the Bank to identify more precisely the socio-economic characteristics of groups
most vulnerable to the economic crisis and social impact of the reform program. This data will
be used to prepare updated poverty lines for Turkey expected to be available by March/April
2002. On the basis of preliminary data, the following vulnerable groups can be tentatively
identified:
* families with children, particularly families with many children.
* single elderly females living alone.
1 Defined as the ratio of laid-off workers to continuing work force.



- 8 -
* widowed/divorced mothers with children (small group).
* families with children in disadvantaged regions: Southern and Eastern Anatolia reflect
high levels of poverty incidence and severity.
* families with children in gecekondu housing.
While not at elevated risk of poverty, educated youth, farmers, and small businessmen are very
much affected by the income declines associated with the crisis. However, falling from middle
class to lower class in Turkey does not mean falling into absolute poverty (living on less than
one US Dollar per person per day) or even chronic poverty. Survey data indicate that more than
half of all female heads of household (where there is no man 'present' in the household) are
married. In these cases, the spouse is presumably working abroad and sending back remittances.
Welfare levels in such households are well above average. The very few households headed by
divorced/widowed mothers or single elderly females are characterized by lower consumption.
17.    Although the PFPSAL program will have important social benefits in terms of restored
growth, job creation and reduced economic vulnerability, structural reforms do entail short-term
social costs which the Government is working to mitigate. Resolution of SDIF banks had
resulted in the loss of some 13,000 jobs as of November 2001. These workers are eligible for
severance payments under the law, but are not eligible for early retirement bonuses.
Restructuring of the state banks has led to a downsizing of staff by some 13,000 workers by the
end of 2001--representing about 20 percent of the workforce--through retirement, voluntary
separation or reassignment. Approximately another 16,000 are expected to leave, or be
transferred from, the state banks by the middle of 2002. These employees are mainly civil
servants and public sector workers who will benefit from relatively generous retirement and
severance payment provisions financed by the Government. Similar provisions apply to those
laid off or retired under programs to adjust staffing levels in the broader public sector (primarily
SEEs), including public sector workers affected by privatization. As part of the fiscal package
for 2002, the Government has lifted restrictions on retirement of public sector workers (although
not civil servants who are subject to specific labor legislation) over the age of 50. This measure
has encouraged roughly 15,000 public sector workers to retire in recent months, mainly in the
SEEs, and additional labor redeployment in the SEE sector is expected during the course of
2002. All of these workers are eligible for pensions and other social insurance benefits. In
addition to these standard benefits, workers laid off from SEEs in the portfolio of the
Privatization Administration benefit from special compensation under the privatization law. All
formal sector workers will benefit from the new unemployment insurance system launched in
mid-2000, once they meet the minimum contribution history requirement of 18 months.
2 According to law no 4603 regarding the state banks, enacted on 25 November 2000, retirement benefits are paid
with an additional 30 percent bonus to those personnel who were eligible for retirement on the date this law was
enacted if they applied for retirement within 6 months of the enactment date and to those personnel who will become
eligible for retirement by the end of 2002 if they apply for retirement within three months of the date they become
eligible. The personnel who are retired on these terms are not allowed to take employment in the state banks for 3
years after their retiremnent. Further legislation was enacted in January allowing the payment of a 20 percent bonus
for those state bank workers who apply for retirement within two months after its enactment. Civil servants and
contract personnel from the state banks also currently have the right to be transferred to another state agency.

18.       The Government has made progress in strengthening Turkey's social protection system
and has successfully maintained social spending under the PFPSAL program. Benchmarks for
public spending on health, education and social protection as shares of GNP were agreed with
the Bank under the first PFPSAL (Table 3). These benchmarks have been set at a level
somewhat above the averages for the 1998-2000 period, reflecting the Government's policy
decision to ensure adequate expenditure on social priorities. The estimated outcome for total
social spending as a share of GNP in 2001 is 15.5 percent, well above the PFPSAL benchmark of
14.5 percent. The individual components of social spending on health, education and social
protection are all also in line with their specific benchmarks as shown in Table 4. In real terms,
total social spending increased by some four percent in 2001, with health and social protection
registering increases of eight percent and seven percent respectively, while education
expenditure fell by three percent in real terms due in part to lower expenditure out of earmarked
revenues from consumption taxes as a result of the recession.
19.       The budget for 2002. approved by Parliament in December. ensures satisfactory
expenditure envelopes for health, education and social protecting, thereby meeting the program
trigger for PFPSAL II. The budget allows for an increase in total social spending to almost 16



- 10-
percent of GNP, an impressive achievement given the programmed fiscal tightening. The 2002
budget maintains public spending on health and social protection as a share of GNP above the
program benchmarks of 3.25 percent and 7.0 percent respectively. Public expenditure on social
protection is budgeted to increase slightly in GNP terms relative to 2001 resulting in part from
the expansion of the direct income support (DIS) program to cover all eligible farmers
nationwide. Per hectare payments under the DIS program for 2002 will be indexed to the 35
percent targeted inflation rate. Expenditure by the Social Solidarity Fund in 2002 will be
sustained at the increased level reached in 2001 (0.3 percent of GNP) with continued support
from the Bank's SRMP. At slightly under 4.0 percent of GNP, public spending on education is
budgeted to fall somewhat below the program benchmark of 4.25 percent of GNP. However, the
Government expects to reallocate additional resources from the budget contingencies to the
education budget to cover the cost of additional teachers to be hired over the course of 2002. As
a result, actual public spending on education is expected to exceed four percent of GNP in 2002.
20.   The preliminary outcome for spending on health, education and social protection under
the 2002 budget will be monitored during the supervision of PFPSAL II. Significant deviations
from the program benchmarks will be discussed with the Bank and appropriate corrective action
taken as needed prior to release of the second and third tranches of the loan. The Government is
committed to allocating satisfactory expenditure envelopes for social programs in the 2003
budget which will be a trigger for the programmatic public sector adjustment loan (PPSAL)
planned under the CAS.
E.    Bank Support for Economic Reform
21.   The proposed PFPSAL II is part of a broader package of Bank financing in support of the
Government's economic reform efforts. The Bank's overall lending program for the 2001-03
period is described in detail in the CAS Progress Report. The CAS includes a high case lending
program of US$5.0 billion plus US$1.2 billion on SSAL terms. The program provides for
adjustment lending of up to US$4.5 billion and up to US$1.7 billion in financing for investment
in support of economic reform and social priorities during the CAS period. The Government's
program meets all of the triRRers for the high case as presented in the CAS Progress Report. In
this context, the tobacco law was enacted on January 9, 2002 which eliminates support purchases
and sets the stage for privatization of TEKEL, the state tobacco company, as foreseen under the
ERL and ARIP programs. In the energy sector, on the basis of a recent Constitutional Court
decision, the Government has decided on a revised roadmap for resolving pending transfer of
operating rights (TOOR) and build-operate-transfer (BOT) projects in order to pave the way for
privatization and the introduction of a competitive electricity market.  The roadmap is
summarized in the attached Letter of Development Policy. These measures are in line with the
CAS high case triggers.
22.   The programmatic lending program continues to meet all of the criteria for both
programmatic and SSAL lending:



- 11 -
*  Progzrammatic Lending.3  The proposed PFPSAL II and follow-up programmatic loans
would continue to support the Government's medium-term reform program which
emphasizes structural, policy and institutional reforms in the financial and public sectors.
This program is designed to respond simultaneously to the current crisis through urgent
financial and fiscal measures, and to prevent a future crisis by tackling the deeper roots of the
chronic financial instability in Turkey. Building on the progress achieved under the first
PFPSAL, the program will help Turkey achieve sustainable poverty reduction through
macroeconomic stability and growth. The program is articulated in detail in the attached
LDP and policy matrix. The triggers and monitoring indicators for the program have been
updated in light of developments(see Part IV). The PFPSAL program remains grounded in
the revised CAS and is closely integrated with other elements of the CAS. The program is
supported by extensive economic sector work including key fiduciary aspects of public
expenditure management. The program's medium-term strategy for financial and public
sector reform helps restore confidence by demonstrating how Turkey will resolve the
underlying structural weaknesses which led to the February crisis.
* SSAL Lendin2i' The provision of US$1.2 billion on SSAL terms over and above the US$5
billion CAS high case is justified by the breadth and severity of the crisis, and its
fundamental structural dimension. The Bank's participation, in concert with the IMF, is
helping the Government articulate its program and is contributing to the restoration of market
confidence, financial health and growth. The Government's structural and macroeconomic
policy package is satisfactory. The 9th and 1 Oth Stand-by program reviews were completed in
August and November 2001, respectively and agreement was reached with the IMF in
December on a revised medium-term macroeconomic framework. The PFPSAL program is
part of a concerted international support package and a satisfactory external financing plan is
in place. The return of private capital inflows, originally projected to start in 2002, has now
been delayed until 2003. In response, the IMF has committed additional financing of US$10
billion in 2002 under the new Stand-by arrangement approved by its Board in February 2002
over and above the amounts committed under the previous Stand-by. The first program
review under the new Stand-by is expected to be completed in April. Under the high case
lending program, IBRD debt outstanding and disbursed would increase to about US$8.5
billion or some 6.6 percent of the total IBRD portfolio in 2003. IBRD exposure and IBRD
debt service as a share of exports of goods and non-factor services and as a share of total
public debt service are projected to remain within guidelines.
23.   The Bank's programmatic assistance in support of financial and public sector reform in
Turkey is a graduated response conditioned on concrete actions taken by the Government. The
programmatic approach recognizes that the complex second generation policy and institutional
reforms that Turkey is undertaking must be sequenced over the medium term. It also takes into
account the uncertainties inherent in the current crisis conditions that Turkey faces and allows for
a reasonable degree of adaptability of the prograrn to unfolding events on the ground. The
See Guidelines for Programmatic Adjustmnent Loans/Credits, OPS, February 11, 2000.
4See Guidelines for Special Structural Adjustment Loans, OPS, April 19, 1999.



- 12-
proposed PFPSAL II is the second operation in support of the Government's multi-year financial
and public sector reform program. It follows on the first PFPSAL of US$1. 1 billion approved in
July 2001. Looking ahead, two Programmatic Financial Sector Adjustment Loans of US$500
million each and a Programmatic Public Sector Adjustment Loan of US$375 million, all on
standard IBRD terms, are envisaged to support continued implementation of the program during
the remainder of the current CAS period. A second PPSAL is tentatively envisaged beyond the
CAS period. The actions taken prior to Board presentation of PFPSAL II and those expected to
be taken by April for release of the second tranche of the loan and by July for release of the third
tranche are specified in the attached policy matrix (Annex VI).
24.   Macro-economic outcomes under the new program will be monitored against key
indicators on a short-term basis in close coordination with the IMEF. The agreed indicators
include the level of domestic interest rates for government securities, inflation, the maturity of
new government borrowing, the cash outcome for the budget, and private capital inflows. The
projections for these indicators have been determined on the basis of the macro-framework
agreed with the IMF. Maintenance of a satisfactory macroeconomic framework. including a
track record of outcomes, will be a condition for release of the second and third tranches of
PFPSAL II.
25.   The PFPSAL programn is integrally linked with other support for the Government's
reform  program  under the revised CAS.  Further implementation of the energy and
telecommunications reforms, and the privatization program, is covered under the conditionality
for the second tranche of the ERL. Mitigation of the social costs of privatization is supported by
the PSSP approved by the Board in December 2000. The ARIP, approved by the Board in July
2001, is assisting the Govermnent in carrying out core aspects of the agriculture reforrn program
originally supported by the ERL including implementation of the DIS program as part of the
broader reform of agriculture support policies. The SRMP approved by the Board in September
is supporting the Government's efforts to strengthen social assistance programs including the
introduction of conditional cash transfers for families at risk. The PSSP and SRMP both include
support for household level survey work to identify more precisely vulnerable groups and track
the social impact of the economic crisis and reform program. In addition, these surveys will
provide information on the distributional impact of the economic recovery. Future Bank support
for basic and secondary education, and for health, will support realization of the objectives of the
broader public sector reform in key social sectors.
26.   The Bank's financial and public sector programnmatic lending operations are supported by
several targeted non-lending activities. A Corporate Sector Assessment to determine the impact
of the crises on the corporate sector and outline a strategy for restructuring will be finalized in
April. A Non-Bank Financial Institutions (NBFI) study planned for 2002 will support design of
the PFSAL I and II programs which will focus on NBFI issues in addition to finalization of the
banking system reform agenda. The content of the public sector reform program draws on
analysis and recommendations presented in the 2000 CEM and the Public Expenditure and
Institutional Review circulated to the Board in September 2001. It also benefits from the Bank's
fiduciary sector work including the Country Procurement Assessment Review (CPAR) finalized
in July 2001. A Country Financial Accountability Assessment (CFAA) was completed and sent
to the Government in August. The Bank has co-sponsored several international conferences and



- 13
workshops to assist the Government in articulating its public sector reform program and in
presenting this program to the public. A new CEM focused on economic recovery and medium-
term growth is planned for the second half of 2002. The Bank is also preparing a report on the
social impact of the crisis and economic reform program, drawing on on-going survey work,
which is expected to be circulated to the Board in 2002. A Local Governtnent Sector Review is
underway which will analyze the key issues faced by sub-national government in Turkey and
selected recommendations of the review may be incorporated into the program for the PPSAL
27.   The PFPSAL program is consistent with the Government's national program for EU
accession and the Bank is working closely with EU staff responsible for Turkey. The banking
sector reforms supported by PFPSAL are designed to ensure that Turkey's banking supervision
framework complies with both EU standards and Basle norms. The Bank has coordinated its
advice on the new public procurement law closely with the EU. The new public procurement
law is compatible with the UNCITRAL model and moves Turkey towards full compliance with
EU directives. The Bank's assistance on public debt management issues has been oriented
towards EU practices and the Maastricht criteria. The Bank is coordinating its dialogue on
financial management issues with the EU, and the new law on public financial management and
financial control is expected to be consistent with EU standards.



- 14-
II.   TURKEY'S FINANCIAL SECTOR REFORM PROGRAM
A.    Introduction
28.   The Government's financial sector reform program seeks to strengthen Turkey's financial
sector, to enhance its intermediation capacity in support of economic growth and to reduce its
vulnerability to internal and external shocks. To achieve this objective, the Government aims to
bring Turkey's legal and regulatory framework for financial sector activity, as well as its
financial institutions, up to Basel/EU and international best practice standards. Because of the
dominance of the banking systern, the initial focus areas of the reform effort, from 1999
onwards, have been: (i) overhaul of the prudential regime for banking; (ii) establishment and
institutional development of a new, independent Bank Regulation and Supervision Agency
(BRSA), (iii) problem bank/bank failure resolution, and (iv) state bank restructuring and
privatization. At a later stage, the reform effort will also encompass non-bank financial
institutions and capital markets activity. The Government's financial sector reform program was
designed with the Bank's assistance in the form of Banking Reviews undertaken in 1998, 1999
and 2001, and Financial Sector Loans in 1999, 2000 and 2001. The Bank's assistance is
described in more detail in Section IV of this report. The paragraphs below describe recent
banking sector developments, the banking sector reforms already undertaken, and the planned
reforms forming the basis for PFPSAL II.
B.    Financial Sector Profile/Recent Developments in the Banking Sector
29.   The Turkish financial sector consists (as of January 2002) of a dominant banking system
of 59 banks. Of these, 44 are deposit taking banks and 15 are non-deposit taking investment and
development banks. Of the 44 deposit taking banks, 3 are state-owned, 4 (formerly privately
owned) are under the Savings Deposit Insurance Fund (SDIF-now quasi state-owned), 22 are
privately Turkish owned and 15 are foreign owned. The banks are complemented by 64
insurance companies, 128 securities trading companies, 216 mutual funds, 100 factoring
companies and 93 leasing companies (several deposit taking and investment and development
banks also hold leasing licenses). There are also several hitherto unregulated private pension
schemes, including schemes run by the banks and insurance companies for their own staff. Most
of the financial institutions are part of financial conglomerates, with the main group commercial
bank at the center of such conglomerates, and other bank and non-bank financial institutions
formed and managed as subsidiaries. Issues related to consolidated regulation and supervision,
financial reporting and comprehensive risk management are increasingly gaining more relevance
because of the prevalence of such group structures. However, even though the non-bank
financial institutions (NBFI) sector has begun to grow in Turkey in terms of size and economic
importance in recent years, and the regulatory oversight and development issues for the NBFI
segment of the financial system are increasing in importance, its size and economic impact
compared to the banking sector are still rather small (see Annex IIA).
30.   Banking Sector: 1990 - November 2000. In the banking sector, the emphasis of the
Government's reform efforts during the nineties was on the development of a basic legal and
regulatory infrastructure to facilitate ease of entry, and to increase competition and growth. This
triggered a rapid expansion of banking system assets in Turkey during the 1990s, and in spite of



- 15 -
the 1994-95 banking crisis, total system assets in absolute terms tripled from US$51.9 billion in
1994 to US$155.2 billion as of end 2000, and also increased substantially as a percentage of
GNP from 39.2 in 1994 to 77.1 in 2000 (Table 4) signaling a deepening of financial
internediation capacity. The rapid growth in the size of the banking sector, however, also had
some negative consequences. Prudential regulations and enforcement capacity lagged behind,
and there was a proliferation of connected lending and concentration of exposure and default
risks. Lenient loan loss provisioning rules and enforcement allowed banks to understate their
credit risks. Large FX open positions and asset/liability mismatches also created foreign currency
and interest rate/liquidity risks in addition to credit risks. Additionally, growing public sector
borrowing requirements during the years 1994-1999 encouraged banks to shift resources into
Government securities that were generating a relatively high risk free real return. As a result,
banks' lending activity declined in real terms.
Table 4: Consolidated Balance Sheet of the Turkish Banking
System, 1991 - September 2001 (US$ millions)

31.     Banking sector: post November 2000. Starting in December 1999, the Government
with the help of the Bank initiated a financial sector reform program to address the above
identified weaknesses (see Section IV A of this report). Despite successful implementation of
these reforms, however, in late November 2000, Turkey experienced a banking system liquidity
crisis which was triggered by the need of a medium-sized private bank (Demir) to refinance an
excessive stock of Government securities. In early December, interest rates briefly spiked to
levels above 1,900 percent. In February 2001, there was another, much more serious crisis that
