was ignited by the public airing of tensions between the President and Prime Minister. This set
off a new wave of turbulence in the financial sector as investors liquidated TL positions and fled
to US$ in expectation of a full fledged political crisis. Interest rates once again spiked, this time
as high as 6,200 percent, and with a rapid depletion of the Central Bank of Turkey's (CBT's)
foreign currency reserves apparent, the authorities were forced to float the TL leading to a sharp
deterioration in the value of the Lira. The combined effect of (i) interest rate losses; (ii) losses
arising from the TL devaluation; and (iii) loan book deterioration losses due to corporate sector
distress resulting from the crises eroded the banking system's capital. Total banking system
assets also have shrunk from US$155.2 billion at the end of December 2000 to US$111.8 billion
at the end of September 2001, a sharp reversal from the trend during 1994-2000. The banking
sector is experiencing financial distress, as is the rest of the economy.
32.    The Government's Response to the Banking Crisis. The Government in response to
the above crises has taken a series of urgent reform actions. These include: (i) significant reform
of prudential regulations for loan loss provisions, connected exposures and foreign exchange
exposures to bring them in line with Basle/EU standards; (ii) strengthening of the new and



- 17 -
independent bank supervision entity (Banking Regulation and Supervision Agency --BRSA);
(iii) restructuring the private banking sector by intervening in 19 insolvent private banks and
undertaking their resolution through sale/merger/or closure; and (iv) initiating the reform of the
state-owned banking sector by closing down the insolvent Emlak bank, and implementing long
overdue financial and governance restructuring of Ziraat and Halk banks. However, while the
above reforms have addressed a significant part of the urgent refonn actions necessary, they have
to be complemented by additional reform actions in all of the above area to sustain the long term
health of the banking sector in Turkey and return it to the growth path. The Government's reform
program has also been supported by the World Bank's Programmatic Financial and Public
Support loans. The first phase of the reforms were supported by PFPSAL in July 2001 and the
second phase is being supported by the proposed PFPSAL II described in this President's Report.
These second phase reforms are described in detail below, and the relationship between the
Government's second phase reform measures, and the program triggers for the proposed
PFPSAL II is outlined in Box 1 below.
C.    Strengthening the Regulatory Framework for Banking
33.   Until recently, Turkey's prudential regime for banking activity was seriously out of line
with Basel/EU standards and international best practice. The weaknesses in the prudential
regime, coupled with rapid banking system growth (Table 4 above), contributed to the
appearance of major vulnerabilities in the banking system to exchange rate, interest rate and
credit risk shocks. These vulnerabilities were already apparent even before the latest two crises,
as evidenced by the emergence of a sizeable pipeline of failed private banks under the SDIF
(Box 2) and the continued financial difficulties experienced by the state-owned banks. The
authorities initiated (from December 1999 onwards) an overhaul of the prudential regime as part
of the program supported by the FSAL and the PFPSAL. Under the proposed PFPSAL II, this
effort will substantially be completed by early 2002, while the following PFSALs I & II in late
2002 and early 2003 will focus on reform of the regulatory regime for non-bank financial
institution activity. Specifically, concerning the prudential regime for banks, the Government
already has undertaken and plans to undertake during late 2001/early 2002 the following
additional reforms.
34.   Loan Loss Provisioning. Reform of the loan loss classification and provisioning regime
for Turkish banks began in December 1999 with the adoption by the Council of Ministers5 of a
new loan loss provisioning rule based on a tiered loan classification system (pass-watch-
substandard-doubtful-loss) and borrower creditworthiness. The regime was further upgraded in
May 2001 through the introduction of full tax deductibility of specific loan loss provisions and
the lifting of the exemption from specific provisioning for Ziraat's agricultural support loans.
These steps were major improvements over the previous, much more lenient regime. However,
the current regime still suffers from the following weaknesses which have become increasingly
apparent since the February 2001 crisis: (i) the absence of fair value accounting rules for
collateral, which has resulted in significant value overstatement and a concomitant
5 The Council of Ministers was responsible for issuing banking regulations before the new BRSA was created in
September 2000.



- 18 -
understatement of provisioning requirements, (ii) overly restrictive provisions concerning the
allowable terms for loan reschedulings, and (iii) a provision in the banking law allowing a four
year phase-in period for classification and provisioning of loans made before June 1999. In
combination, these factors have discouraged Turkish private banks from restructuring problem
loans, while at the same time allowing them to significantly understate their non-performing
loans (NPLs). For the private banks, NPLs as a percent of total loans have remained at around 5
percent despite the major upheaval in the corporate sector caused by the crises. Such a low NPL
ratio, also as compared with the NPL experience of other major crisis countries in Asia where
NPL ratios reached 40 percent. has resulted in a significant overstatement of bank capital6. To
remedy these weaknesses and to set the stage for a more constructive and informed approach
towards problem loan workout and restoration of banks' capital adequacy ratios, the Government
has submitted, and Parliament has enacted, a banking law amendment eliminating the four year
phase-in period for pre-June 1999 loans. The law was published in the official gazette as of
January 31. 2002. Additionally. the BRSA as of January 31. 2002 issued a supplementary
memorandum to the loan loss provisioning rule to clarify fair value accounting rules for
collateral, and also amended the LLP rule itself to remove the restrictive debt rescheduling
provisions. At the same time, the BRSA will ensure that rescheduled loans are not upgraded to a
next higher classification level until the debtor concerned has firmly established a new,
satisfactory debt service track record and renewed creditworthiness.
35.   Large/Connected Exposures. The Turkish banking system during the past decade has
been characterized by a high degree of connected lending and equity exposures, as group-owned
banks financed group companies with both bank loans and equity investments. The authorities
have recognized the need to overhaul the prudential regime for such connected exposures, and
have already implemented the necessary reforms in this area. The December 1999 banking law
introduced a new, lower limit for connected exposures by banks and a six year phase-in period
for banks to reach the new limit in a step-wise fashion by the end of 2006. In April 2001, the
Parliament enacted a further amendment to the law separating connected lending & derivative
exposures from equity exposures (i.e., equity ownership in non-financial entities), and
introducing a new separate limit for such non-financial equity exposures. This new limit, which
is consistent with the applicable EU Directive, will be phased in over a period of 8 I/2 years, to be
in full compliance with EU non-financial equity exposure levels by 2009. Additionally, the latest
banking law amendment also mandates the application of the exposure limits (for connected
lending/derivatives and non-financial equity) on both a solo and consolidated basis (i.e., to banks
and their financial subsidiaries together). In June 2001 the BRSA also issued the new, EU
Directive based definition of connected exposures called for by the banking law, as well as the
interim targets and time-table for banks to come stepwise into compliance with the new non-
financial equity exposure limits.
36.   Although the new connected exposure regime allows a long phase-in period for banks to
reach compliance with the new lower limits (all expressed as a percent of bank capital), several
banks cannot meet the higher, interim limits for 2001/2002. This is in part due to the fact that the
6 The state-owned banks Ziraat and Halk, however, as well as the SDIF banks, under instructions from the
Government and the BRSA/SDIF, are maintaining much more realistic provisioning levels.



- 19 -
banks' capital base has shrunk as a result of the crises. In addition, some existing violations have
worsened because foreign lenders (or even domestic banks trying to reduce exposures to other
groups) have recalled loans to Turkish banks/enterprises, causing group banks to maintain or
even increase their group exposures to replace such creditors. To enforce compliance, the BRSA
has issued a regulation defining time-bound reduction targets for all non-compliant banks and
will actively monitor compliance with the reduction targets. The BRSA will develop and enter
into bank-specific corrective action plans for any bank not meeting the targets. For
undercapitalized banks, these plans will be an integral part of capital restoration efforts (see
paragraph 53-54 below). Also, where necessary, enforcement action will include restructuring of
financial industrial groups into separate financial and industrial holding companies (see
paragraph 40 below).
37.    Foreign Exchange (FX) Exposure. Weaknesses in the FX exposure rules for banks
were a major concern during the past few years, when banks were routinely carrying large short
open FX positions to arbitrage the Turkish Lira-US$ interest rate differential. To mitigate the
impact on bank capital of large unexpected exchange rate shocks, efforts to tighten the FX
exposure limits were initiated during 1998-99 when the maximum open FX position limit for
banks was reduced in a stepwise fashion from 50 percent of regulatory capital in June 1998 to 20
percent in October 1999 7. In spite of the tighter rules, however, loopholes remained and banks -
having taken advantage of such possible loopholes to carry excessive FX exposures - incurred
large losses due to the February 2000 TL devaluation. Even though most banks have since
sharply reduced their FX exposures, any such loopholes should nevertheless be closed to avoid
recurrence of abuse in future. A working group comprising BRSA and CBT staff has been set up
to: (i) catalogue the use by banks of structured finance products to incur FX exposures not
captured by existing regulations, and (ii) formulate remedial regulatory action to capture such
exposures.
38.    Repurchase Transactions. Turkey is unique in having a significant retail repurchase
and reverse repurchase agreement (repo) market. Repos are typically transacted on a wholesale
basis between banks, other financial institutions or large corporate treasuries for liquidity
management purposes. Prior to December 2000, in order to encourage secondary market
liquidity, the repo market enjoyed more favorable withholding tax treatment than deposit interest
and made repos attractive instruments for retail customers. Following an amendment of the
withholding tax rules in December 2000, banks primarily entered into repo contracts with their
retail clients to circumvent the CBT's reserve requirement on deposits. In February 2001, as repo
interest rates rose to as high as 6,200 percent per annum, a large numbers of customers cancelled
time deposits and moved into the highly lucrative repo market. The movement of funds from
7 In December 1999 the limit also was applied on both a solo and consolidated basis. A new BRSA regulation
imposing a Basel-compliant capital charge on banks for their open FX positions was issued in February 2001,
effective January 1, 2002 on a solo basis and July 1, 2002 on a consolidated basis. Also, in June 2001 the BRSA
amended the solo and consolidated capital adequacy and FX exposure rules increasing the reporting frequency from
quarterly to monthly. Furthermore, the BRSA has undertaken on-site examninations of the foreign subsidiaries of
Turkish banks abroad in coordination with foreign supervisors to verify Turkish banks' compliance with
consolidated FX open position limits (e.g., in Bahrain, Luxemburg, Cyprus and the Netherlands).



- 20 -
time deposits to the overnight repo market obliged some banks to borrow more heavily on the
inter-bank market, further destabilizing the banking system.
39.   As a first step to curb repo activity, the Government in July 2001 created a committee
comprising of representatives of the Treasury, the BRSA, the CBT, the Capital Markets Board
(CMB) and the Ministry of Finance (MOF) to recommend measures to reduce the risks caused
by excessive use of retail repos/short-term liabilities in the banking sector. This committee
completed its work in early August, and the Council of Ministers issued a decree8 on August 17,
2001 introducing a new withholding tax regime for deposits and repos with a view to encourage
a migration of funds from FX to TL, from off balance sheet repo transactions to on-balance sheet
deposits and from short term to longer term deposits. Also, the CBT introduced 40 percent
remuneration on hitherto unremunerated mandatory TL reserve requirements payable as of
September 1, 2001. There is preliminary evidence that these measures are already having some
effect, as evidenced by a decline in outstanding retail repos. Additionally. to further enhance the
transparency of the repo market, the BRSA issued a new regulation on December 13. 2001 with
applicability as of February 1. 2002 requiring banks to bring repos on balance sheet as
collateralized finance transactions.
40.    Taxation and Corporate Law    Reform   to Encourage Mergers/De-mergers and
Facilitate Separation of Financial Industrial Groups. To develop a healthy, strong and
modern Turkish banking sector, it is necessary to encourage mergers and consolidation among
the large number of small banks without the requisite size, capital, range of products and
services, and technical and managerial depth. At the same time, to facilitate efficient financial
flows among financial entities within a single business group, and to better manage and
supervise the inherent risks on a consolidated basis for group financial institutions, it is necessary
to separate the financial and industrial activities of the financial-industrial groups (FIGs) in
Turke . The Parliament has already enacted several amendments to tax legislation that facilitate
bank mergers. However, the Ministry of Finance still needs to issue an implementation decree to
operationalize the new tax provisions. Also, further amendments to tax laws as well as company
and financial sector legislation may still be necessary to allow full-scale corporate restructuring
(including de-mergers) of mixed conglomerates into separate financial and industrial holding
company structures: During 2002, as part of the action plans to reduced connected and large
exposures of banks to their/or other FIGs (see paragraph 35 above), the BRSA will recommend
restructuring of such intertwined financial/industrial conglomerates into separate financial and
industrial holding entities. In this context, BRSA will, in cooperation with the MOF and other
ministries and regulatory agencies, seek to remove any remaining legal/tax impediments.
8 The decree introduced the following withholding tax rates: (i) for FX deposits: for deposits up to one year 18
percent, for deposits longer than one year 16 percent; (ii) for TL deposits: for demand deposits 16 percent, time
deposit up to 3 months 16 percent, time deposit up to 6 months 14 percent, time deposit up to one year 10 percent,
time deposits longer than one year 6 percent; and (iii) for repos 20 percent. These withholding tax rates are effective
for deposit accounts opened or renewed, and repos entered into, after August 17, 2001.



- 21 -
D.    Strengthening the Institutional Capacity of the BRSA and the SDIF
41.   BRSA. Shortly after its operationalization on September 1, 2000 the BRSA identified
the following institutional development priorities: (i) integration of staff coming to the BRSA
from five different departments within the Treasury, the MOF and the CBT, and staff training;
(ii) development of a common database for all supervisory and enforcement departments (e.g.,
offsite monitoring, on-site supervision, enforcement, licensing) within the BRSA; (iii) upgrading
of existing procedures manuals; (iv) integration of offsite monitoring & on-site supervision and
enforcement activity to improve the effectiveness of the bank regulatory regime despite the fact
that the Sworn Bank Auditors have a special status under the banking law; (v) establishment of a
problem bank function within the enforcement department and development of manuals to deal
with problem banks; (vi) introduction of an early warning system to guide the efforts of onsite
examiners and provide input in determining the onsite examination cycle; and (vi) development
of the concept of risk-based supervision, to shift the onsite examiners from being mostly focused
on compliance testing to identifying banking risks and assessing the quality of the risk
management undertaken by bank managers.
42.   Institutional Development Plan Adopted and Implementation Underway. To address
these institutional development priorities, the BRSA Board adopted a time-bound institutional
development plan during its June 2001 meeting. The plan clearly assigned responsibilities for its
implementation. Implementation progress has been slower than planned, however, as most of
senior management's attention and scarce human resources have been devoted to resolving
problem banks and taking actions to stabilize the financial system in the aftermath of the crises.
The implementation status of the plan's major components is described below. Satisfactory
progress in all these areas is expected to be achieved in the first half of 2002.
43.   HR Policy-Staff Integration, Training and Staff Salaries. The BRSA has implemented
most of the actions needed to integrate staff from different sources. Coordination of work and
information flow between departments has improved. BRSA employees also have taken part in
various external training programs. However, the development of a human resource policy and
an internal training function has not yet been completed. Besides, a comprehensive review of the
salary and remuneration of BRSA staff to bring the salaries of BRSA Board members and staff
in line with the prevailing salary scales for similar professional staff in the banking sector (in any
event not less than comparable salaries at the CBT or CMB), is also still to be carried out. In July
2001, the Government issued instructions freezing BRSA staff salaries, causing them to drop
significantly below CBT salaries, and triggering the resignation of most of the CBT staff
seconded to the BRSA. Staff morale has gone down as a result. In order to rectify this situation,
BRSA HR staff remuneration policy consistent with the independent professional nature of
BRSA has to be developed.
44.   Common Database. With full access to the CBT's data base being provided to all
departments, the practice of using separate data bases by different departments has ended. The
BRSA is presently discussing with the CBT and the Banks' Association of Turkey how existing
regulatory reports can be streamlined to avoid duplications, while ensuring that its regulatory
requirements are met. Once this process is completed, a decision will be taken to determine
whether the present CBT data base best serves the BRSA's needs and therefore should be
retained, or whether it would be more beneficial to develop a new data base.



-22 -
45.   Problem Bank Function. The BRSA has created a problem bank unit within its
enforcement department, and is developing a manual for the new unit, which will define pre-
failure intervention triggers (effectively defining a 'problem bank') and pre-failure corrective
action procedures to be administered by the new unit. The BRSA has sent the draft manual to the
Bank and the manual will be finalized after receiving the Bank's comments. The upgrading of
procedures manuals for all other existing BRSA units is progressing well and should be
completed by year end 2002.
46.   Integration of offsite & onsite monitoring and enforcement. Although significant progress
has been made in information sharing between departments, the concept of relationship
supervision with offsite examiners, enforcement staff, and Swom Bank auditors working in
teams on individual banks is not yet in place, and is hindered by banking law provisions
specifying that only Sworn Bank Auditors can examine banks. Informal discussions do take
place between departments to discuss bank issues but there is a further need to integrate sworn
bank auditors with the rest of the supervision staff within BRSA to develop a "team supervision"
approach.
47.   SDIF. In September 2000 the SDIF (responsible for administering the deposit insurance
scheme and for intervening and resolving failed banks), was transferred from the CBT where it
was housed to the new BRSA. The SDIF was already dealing with a sizeable pipeline of failed
banks before this transfer, and the case load of the agency has only increased since then.
Additionally, immediately after the November 2000 crisis, the SDIF was also given the
responsibility by the Government to administer the blanket guarantee covering all bank
depositors and creditors (except subordinated debt holders). To ensure the SDIF can effectively
implement its multiple functions, it is in need of a targeted institutional development effort. Key
development priorities include:
*  Overhaul of the SDIF's organizational structure to more clearly assign responsibility for
(i) the provision of overall strategic guidance and coordination, (ii) interim management
and resolution of intervened banks and (iii) asset resolution;
* Development of an asset resolution strate , including: (i) drawing up an inventory of
assets received, (ii) grouping them into various categories based on the appropriate
resolution/asset management/recovery method for each category; and (iii) creating an
appropriate institutional capacity including procedures, manuals and staff training; and
* Relocation. Most of the SDIF's activities take place in Istanbul, while the organization is
divided between Ankara and Istanbul;
* Funding. The SDIF resources from premiums collected from banks (even though
inadequate in the short term to meet the blanket guarantee commitments), have to be
properly planned to meet future deposit insurance commitments.
48.   SDIF management has realized that it needs a comprehensive institutional development
plan to address the above identified institutional development priorities. The SDIF has agreed to
seek external technical assistance from reputed consulting firms for this purpose, and has
selected McKinsey to provide such advisory services. With the help of the McKinsey technical



- 23 -
assistance primarily on the organization of the asset management and resolution activities, the
SDIF management will prepare a full institutional development plan by April 2002.
49.   SDIF Financial Statements and Audit. In view of the large number of bank interventions
undertaken by the SDIF and the complexity of the financial transactions used to resolve
intervened banks, the SDIF Board has decided to prepare detailed year-end 2001 financial
statements and to hire an independent external audit firm to audit these statements. A proper
financial track record can be used by the SDIF in future to raise public funds from local and
international markets, thereby reducing its dependence on Government resources to fund any
large commitments. The audit report is expected to be available by end July 2002 at the latest.
50.   Deposit Insurance Reform. The institutional development of the SDIF cannot be
successfully completed until its long term mandate is clearly articulated. In this context, over
time, the Government plans to remove the blanket guarantee for all bank depositors and creditors
presently in force under crisis conditions, and replace it with a risk-based deposit insurance
regime consistent with the applicable EU Directive. At the same time, the effective functioning
of the SDIF's present legal, governance, financing and management structure as a subsidiary of
the BRSA will be reviewed, and the feasibility of the SDIF existing as a separate autonomous
and institution will be considered as necessary. These further reform measures for the SDIF are
envisaged under the planned PFSAL I and II during 2002/and 2003.
E.    Implementation of Efficient Problem Bank/Bank Failure Resolution
51.   In the aftermath of the November 2000 and February 2001 crises, the capital base of
nearly all of the private, domestically owned deposit taking banks (21 banks as of early
December 2001 excluding SDIF banks) has been weakened substantially. Stress testing has
confirmed that as a result of the combined impact of interest rate, exchange rate and credit risk
shocks, many of the banks concerned either already have suffered, or are likely to suffer, from
capital inadequacy in the near future. Banks' capital deficits range from temporary non-
compliance with the eight percent minimum capital adequacy ratio requirement to deep
insolvency and inability of some of the banks concerned to survive on their own. To remedy this
potentially systemic capital shortfall, during the period March-May, 2001 the BRSA undertook
the following actions:
* The BRSA instructed all undercapitalized private banks not to pay dividends and to retain
all year 2000 earnings;
* Through a combination of offsite analysis and on-site examinations, the BRSA identified
all capital deficient banks, and the likely size of the already apparent, or soon to be
apparent capital shortfall in each bank concerned;
* The BRSA held in-depth discussions with each bank concerned on time-bound capital
restoration plans, including in some instances the need for the merger of multiple weaker
banks that belong to the same financial-industrial group into one larger, stronger bank;
and
* By end July 2001, the BRSA entered into capital restoration plans with all capital
deficient banks with the aim to restore their capital adequacy ratio to eight percent as
soon as possible.



- 24 -
52.   Continued Financial and Corporate Distress Further Erodes Banks' Capital. Under
their capital restoration plans, a number of private bank owners did put significant new capital
(nearly US$1.3 billion) into their banks. However, these amounts have been inadequate in light
of a broad and deep loss of capital in the private banking sector over 2001. The deep recession in
the second half of 2001, coupled with high interest rates and exchange rate depreciation, has
caused widespread corporate and financial sector distress, causing a steep increase in the level of
non- performing loans (NPLs) in the banking sector. NPLs in private banks' formal financial
statements are significantly understated (around 5 percent), while they have been more
accurately recorded in SDIF banks (nearly 50 percent) and state-owned banks (nearly 20
percent). Keeping in mind the level of NPLs during the East Asia crises, and the current
estimated levels of NPLs in the banking systems of Indonesia, China and Japan, it appears likely
that the level of NPLs in Turkish private banks is much higher than what is officially stated. At
the same time, the market value of collateral securing these loans has also fallen considerably
due to lack of demand for assets. The BRSA will undertake the necessary regulatory and
enforcement actions, including instructing external auditors to launch the process of ensuring
that all non-performing loans are correctly and transparently stated through proper classification
and provisioning procedures in the private deposit taking banks' balance sheets in order to reflect
their true capital position. However, there is general consensus that a significant number of
banks continue to be undercapitalized, and that the capacity of private bank owners to further
capitalize their banks is now somewhat limited.
53.   Strategy for Undercapitalized Banks at the end of December 2001. Private banks'
capital shortfalls, coupled with an aversion to risk based financial intermediation to the corporate
sector under unstable and difficult economic circumstances, has led the BRSA and the
Government to discuss possible strategies for restructuring the private banking sector. These
include: (i) facilitating development of an informal and voluntary corporate debt restructuring
framework under the auspices of the Banks' Association of Turkey (called the "Istanbul
Approach" on the lines of the "London Approach") funded by the banking sector's own
resources; and (ii) exploring options for possible public solvency support by the Government
through the SDIF for severely affected banks in order to retain a reasonable level of financial
intermediation in the sector.
54.   A corporate debt restructuring framework is necessary (either an informal approach or a
formal approach under the bankruptcy law), with the following sequence: (i) full recognition of
NPLs and capital losses in banks as the first step; (ii) capital restructuring of banks and
ownership and management changes/adjustments as a result of new capital holdings as a second
step; and (iii) corporate debt restructuring to be undertaken by a healthy banks ownership and
management team as a third step. This process will help Turkey achieve the required goals of
proper re-engagement between banks and their clients in the real sector. A public solvency
support scheme to assist in the re-capitalization of private banks has been planned by the
government and a "private bank re-capitalization law" explaining the rules and criteria for such
assistance has been passed in the Parliament on January 11, 2002 and with President's approval
became effective on January 31, 2002. Subsequent to this law, detailed regulations have also
been issued by the BRSA regarding (i) the norms for the detailed portfolio audits to be carried
out in the private banks by the external auditors to determine the correct levels of non performing



- 25 -
loans, and therefore the correct level of capital in the private banks; and (ii) the procedures for
implementation of the re-capitalization program. The audits are expected to be launched by the
early February 2002, and the first round of audit results are expected to be completed by the end
of March 2002. These audit results will be verified by a second set of external auditors by
approximately end April 2002. Based on these findings, by end May, 2002, the BRSA will
determine the correct level of capital in the private banks and finalize the re-capitalization
assistance program. The re-capitalization program implementation is expected to be carried
during June 2002.
55.    SDIF Banks. The resolution of SDIF banks began in earnest after the creation of the
BRSA in September 2000 and the transfer of the SDIF to the BRSA. During 2001, in addition to
stepped-up efforts to sell banks as going concems, resulting in the sale of Ekspress, Sumer and
Demir, the SDIF initiated a consolidation strategy through merger of groups of banks under its
control. As a result, by early November 2001 only eight banks remained under SDIF control In
late November Toprak Bank was also intervened by SDIF. However, subsequently during
December 2001- January 2002, the licenses of Eti, Iktisat and Kent were revoked and the banks
were put under liquidation, Site was sold to the Nova bank of Greece, EGS was merged with
Bayinder, thereby reducing the number of banks with SDIF under resolution to two-Bayindir
and Toprak. Out of the previously intervened banks, Turk Ticaret and Taris shareholders have
filed lawsuits in the Administrative courts and their final resolution will depend on the resolution
of the court appeals (see Box 2 below).
56.   Residual Liabilities and Assets-Last SDIF Bank. However, in the above resolution
process, while banks were being sold/ merged/closed, there still remained the problem of
managing residual liabilities and assets. Because the buyers of Ekspress, Sumer and Demir were
interested in acquiring only a small part of the assets and liabilities, especially FX liabilities, by
early November 2001 the SDIF was still left with approximately US$10.6 billion equivalent in
liabilities. Under the SDIF resolution program agreed with the Bank and the IMF, all SDIF banks
intervened before November 2001 were to be sold/merged/closed by end 2001. To meet this
deadline, during December 2001 the SDIF sold US$2.9 billion (US$2.6 billion in FX and
US$0.3 billion in TL) of SDIF bank liabilities to other private banks through a series of auctions,
together with a corresponding amount of Government securities based on the auction yield.
Additional FX liabilities and assets worth US$2.4 billion were also transferred to Ziraat and Halk
in late November 2001. All remaining deposit liabilities and non deposit liabilities together with
corresponding amounts of assets were transferred to Toprak (intervened in late November 2001)
and Bayindir. Bayindir has been restricted from taking new deposits and its activities in future
will focus on completion of asset resolution.



- 26 -
Box 2: SDIF Banks
During the period 1997-early 1999 only 3 banks were intervened by the SDIF. In September 1999, recognizing that problems were
mounting in the banking sector, an intensive dialogue between the Govemment and the Bank was initiated, resulting in the enactment of a
new banking law in December 1999 with strong resolution powers for the SDIF. Immediately upon the passage of the new law, the SDIF
intervened in an additional 5 banks. This rapid phase of initial action was followed by a 9 month period of relative inaction, as the CBT
and the Treasury were waiting for the fornation of the new BRSA. Unresolved questions about how the SDIF was to finance the injection
of cash and Govemment securities required for resolution of the banks under its control also contributed to the inaction.

With the operationalization of the BRSA on September 1, 2000 the pace of intervention and resolution picked up. In October 2000, an
additional 2 banks were intervened. In November 2000, the SDIF re-capitalized the banks under its control and immediately afterwards
announced the sale of the banks. Though there was some initial interest from both domestic and intemational groups, no banks were sold.
The November 2000 banking crisis was triggered by the excessive liquidity demands of Demir, which needed funds to support a highly
speculative position in Govemment securities. Unable to meet its liabilities as they fell due, Demir was taken over by the SDIF. To
prevent a system-wide deposit run, the Govemment issued a blanket guarantee, to be administered by the SDIF and exercisable upon
SDIF intervention, covering all banking system liabilities (except subordinated debt). Immediately following the February 2001 banking
crisis, the SDIF intervened in an additional 2 banks. Six more banks have been intervened since (five in July and one in November). The
principal features of most of these bank failures have been acute shortages of liquidity and a high incidence of fraudulent/criminal activity
on the part of shareholders and management.
Unable to dispose of the banks as going concems, in late 1999 the SDIF revoked the licenses of 5 banks and merged them into a
transition bank (Sumer). After financial and operational restructuring, in August 2001 Sumer was sold to Oyak, the Turkish army's
pension fund. A second transition bank was fomied by merging Inter and Es into Eti and the licenses of the former two banks were
revoked. The merged entity was restructured and offered for sale, but no buyers were found and its license was revoked on December 28,
2001. Two other banks were sold (Ekspress in June to Tekfen Holdings, and Demir in October to HSBC). In all three sales transactions,
however, only a part of assets and liabilities was sold. To meet the end December 2001 deadline of resolving all banks intervened prior to
November 2001, the SDIF during late 2001 auctioned off US$2.9 billion of SDIF bank liabilities together with matching assets to private
banks, transferred US$2.4 billion of FX assets and liabilities to Ziraat and Halk, and moved the remaining assets and liabilities to Toprak
(intervened in late November 2001) and Bayindir. Bayinder has been restricted from taking new deposits and its activities will be
focused in future on completion of asset resolution.



- 27 -
F.     Legal Framework for Creditor Rights, Bankruptcy and Debt Workout
57.    As Turkey is currently facing one of its most severe financial crises with large amounts
of NPLs on the books of commercial banks, effective NPL resolution through efficient legal
mechanisms for debt recovery, insolvency and informal workouts is a critical necessity.
However, in Turkey, NPL resolution is constrained by inadequate and outdated legal procedures,
and institutional inefficiencies. Effective NPL resolution requires: (i) strengthening creditor
protection; (ii) accelerating bankruptcy resolution through the courts and (iii) creating debtor
rehabilitation/workout mechanisms both under the bankruptcy law, and through informal debt
resolution among debtors and multiple lenders ('Istanbul' approach, modeled on the London
approach, also used by Asian crisis countries). The Government already has prepared new
legislation for informal debt resolution that it intends to submit to Parliament shortly. Some
necessary amendments to the civil code and the bankruptcy law are also already planned by the
Ministry of Justice. Recognizing the need for greater efficiency in the court system, the
Government furthermore recently also already prepared new legislation to create an intermediate
court of appeals. But much more comprehensive legal and institutional reforms will be needed,
and to this end the Government has agreed to undertake an overall assessment of its legal and
institutional framework for creditor rights and insolvency, using the World Bank's Principles
and Guidelines for Effective Insolvency and Creditor Rights Systems (April 2001). This
assessment will identify specific reforms required in Turkey's legal and institutional framework
in core areas related to enforcement of creditor rights (including creation, registration and
enforcement of security), formal and informal corporate insolvency and restructuring systems,
credit risk management practices and institutional and regulatory frameworks. The Government
is committed to subsequently pursue implementation of the actions for legal reform and capacity
building recommended by the assessment. Specifically, the Ministry of Justice plans to establish
a feedback group to review and provide input to the assessment and develop a strategy for
corrective measures to strengthen relevant legislation. The Ministry of Justice also plans to
establish a commission to prepare amendments to relevant legislation for submission to
Parliament as soon as possible in an effort to maximize prospects for resolution of non-
performing loans and to kick start the credit markets.
G.     Implementation of Comprehensive Restructuring and Privatization of State-owned
Banks
58.    Background. Until July 2001, nearly a third of banking system assets and deposits were
accounted for by four large state-owned banks, Ziraat, Halk, Emlak and Vakif, with Ziraat
traditionally focused on agricultural finance9, Halk on lending to small businesses and artisans,
and Emlak on public housing construction. Vakif is a retail and general corporate sector focused
bank, and has an ownership structure dating from Ottoman times, with A and B shares (55 and
20 percent of capital respectively) historically belonging to so-called 'vakifs' (foundations) and
9 Ziraat actually combines three separate activities into one entity: (i) agricultural finance; (ii) general commnercial
banking; and (iii) payment and collection services for the Government. Because of the latter, and as the bank is the
only bank serving small rural communities, its restructuring and privatization will be more complex than that of the
other three state banks. In preparation for its eventual privatization, Ziraat prepared its first set of IAS financial
statements for the year ended December 1999.



- 28 -
now represented by the Ministry of State's General Directorate of Foundations, and the
remaining C shares (25 percent of total) belonging to the bank's pension fund and employees.
Longstanding Government governance and ownership steadily eroded the financial condition of
Ziraat, Halk and Emlak. For Ziraat and Halk, enormous losses (nearly US$23.0 billion as of
April 200110) were hidden under the umbrella of illiquid Government "duty losses" (paper
claims on the Government carried on the banks' books) as a by-product of subsidized, directed
lending for agriculture and small business development. The distortion in the money markets
caused by the large liquidity shortfalls experienced by these two banks as a result of the rapid
build-up of the duty loss claims was one of the principal propagators of the November 2000 and
February 2001 banking crises. Similarly, Emlak also accumulated significant losses through its
construction related activities and non-performing loans, and the bank was already insolvent
even before the November 2000 crisis.
59.   Financial and Governance Restructuring-First Phase Already Completed. To put
the financial structure of the state-owned banks on a sound footing, to ensure that these banks are
effectively and professionally managed on an arms length basis and transformed into profitable
commercial banks that can be rapidly privatized, the Government, in cooperation with the CBT
and the BRSA, has undertaken the following actions:
* recapitalization of Ziraat and Halk with Government securities at market terms in a mix
of maturities and currency denominations with floating coupon interest paid quarterly to
ensure that the banks have sufficient liquidity to operate and honor any deposit
withdrawals, allow the write-off of all duty loss claims as of mid May 2001 and the
absorption of losses associated with the merger of Emlak into Ziraat (see below);
*  appointment of a new independent professional Governing Board for the two banks with
the mandate to manage these banks in accordance with commercial principles and all
prudential guidelines under the banking law/issued by the BRSA; the Governing Board
has initiated the operational restructuring of the two banks, including the closure of
unprofitable branches and the reduction of redundant staff;
* appointment by the Governing Board of a Restructuring Adviser for Ziraat and a
Restructuring and Privatization Preparation Adviser for Halk;
* the transfer of construction related assets on the books of Emlak to the Mass Housing
Administration, the completion of the merger of all banking liabilities and all the
performing banking assets of Emlak with Ziraat; the provision of sufficient capital to
Ziraat in the form of marketable Government securities carrying quarterly floating rate
coupon interest to allow it to absorb all losses related to Emlak's merger; and
* the revocation of Emlak's banking license (July 2001).
60.   Financial and Operational Restructuring-Second Phase Underway. The creation of
a joint governing Board for Ziraat and Halk with a common chairman has significantly improved
governance of the two banks. Both banks are now managed by a single management board
responsible for all policy decisions on strategy, credit, asset/liability management, management
information system, human resource management and so on.
10 Further details are available in the PFPSAL Memorandum of the President dated June 2001.



- 29 -
61.   Financial Restructuring. The first priority of the management has been to put the two
banks on a sound financial footing. Towards this objective the two banks are now: (i) capitalized
significantly over the minimum capital adequacy ratios; (ii) FX risk has been eliminated by
matching short term FX assets with short term FX liabilities; (iii) the banks' liquidity position
has steadily improved through receipt of coupoIn interest on Government securities in cash, and
short term TL repos are gradually being eliminated-management has already eliminated short
term TL repos in Ziraat during December 2001, and will do so in Halk by end May 2002; (iv)
liability interest costs have been brought down through good liability management, and as a
result operating earning margins have turned significantly positive for Ziraat while they are still
borderline for Halk; and (v) better management of the credit portfolio and recognition of NPLs,
and stopping making loans to non-deserving entities. It is important to note that in spite of tighter
credit management, loans to small farmers and entrepreneurs continue to be made more or less as
before--it is the provision of larger loans to unsound borrowers for non-commercial reasons that
has now been stopped.
62.   Impact of Government Plans to Capitalize Interest and Principal Payments on
Government Securities held by Ziraat and Halk. Treasury is considering to capitalize some of the
coupon interest and principal payments due to Ziraat and Halk rather than to pay in cash. This
may cause: (i) a ballooning of Ziraat's and Halk's Government securities portfolios, with
concomitant enhanced liquidity and interest rate risks, and (ii) a reversal of the financial
restructuring process currently underway. To mitigate these risks, Treasury has assured
management that it will provide sufficient coupon interest and principal payments in cash
(approximately TL 8.3 quadrillion during 2002) to: (i) allow elimination of all short term
liabilities; and (ii) accommodate a certain risk level of term deposits not being rolled over by
depositors (a roll-over ratio of 75 percent is assumed). Treasury and management are in broad
agreement with this strategy, and so far the coordination between Treasury and the two banks has
worked well.
63.   Operational restructuring. The operational restructuring of the two banks in terms of
organizational structure, introduction of the concept of profit centers, reduction of the branch
networks and staffing etc. has begun, but has met with some resistance, especially on the issue of
branch closures. The details are:
64.   Branch network. Management has put in place a new branch structure for the two banks,
which consists of 37 hub branches at Ziraat, and 22 hub branches at Halk. The objective is to
enable the hubs to function as profit centers with responsibility for the resources and business in
their respective regions. In addition to the hubs, management estimates that Ziraat should have
around 1,000 sub-branches and Halk around 500 for profitable operation. Taking into account
the current number of branches of the two banks (including branches inherited from Emlak), this
implies that nearly 900 branches will need to be closed by mid 2002. To date (January 2002) 96
Ziraat and Halk branches have been closed, but these closures were in areas where there was
duplication. Further closures have been put on hold pending resolution of political objections to
the social upheavals such closures might cause.
65.   Staffing. Ziraat currently (January 2002) has around 33,000 staff, and Halk 15,000-
inclusive of about 9,000 former employees of Emlak. Management estimates that the number of



-30-
employees should be reduced to approximately 21,000 in Ziraat and 11,000 in Halk by mid 2002,
a substantial downsizing of nearly 16,000 employees within half a year. All employees, based on
new contracts containing job descriptions and associated remuneration will be given a limited
time period in which to either (i) accept the position, (ii) leave the bank (including possibly
through retirement), (iii) or choose to remain civil servants to be transferred to other Government
agencies (this will require issuance of Government instructions to facilitate such transfers). This
process has commenced with the top 6,000 staff already contracted under private law in the two
banks (January 2002) and will continue over the coming six months.
66.   To overcome hurdles to branch closures and staffing reductions, the Council of Ministers
issued a decree no. 2002/3555 dated January 12, 2002, approving management's proposed
course of action. Without such backing it would not have been possible to carry out the
operational restructuring within the timeframe envisaged. As some branch closures may result in
unavailability of banking service in certain rural areas of the country, the Post Office
Administration has entered into a Protocol with the Social Security Institutions, SSK and Emekli
Sandigi to make payments on behalf of them.
67.   Privatization of Halk and Ziraat-Third Phase. As noted above, the financial and
operational restructuring of the two banks should be largely completed by end 2002. Therefore,
preparation for the two banks' privatization should start now to allow the sale of the two banks
upon completion of their restructuring. For this purpose, the Government plans to appoint a
privatization advisor for the two banks by mid 2002. Completion of the necessary pre-
privatization actions for both banks are expected to be mostly completed by end 2002, and Halk
is expected to be privatized in the first half of 2003. Ziraat also plans to complete, by mid 2002, a
study on the future strategic direction of agricultural finance in Turkey and Ziraat's role in the
provision of such finance. A sale of shares in Ziraat to private sector entities resulting in majority
private ownership and control (more than 51 percent) is expected during 2003.
68.   Privatization of Vakif. In June 2001, Parliament enacted an amendment to the law on
the privatization of Vakif to allow the simultaneous sale of A and B shares. The Government and
Vakif management have now decided to proceed with a joint sale of the balance 75 percent (both
A and B) shares of Vakif still under state ownership. Vakif management has hired an investment
bank to act as Privatization Advisor for the sale. Following a short listing and bidding process
involving reputable international investment banks, Vakif has appointed Merrill Lynch for this
purpose. The process of seeking expressions of interest from prospective buyers and of
undertaking due diligence has started. Vakif, because of its strong client base and a diversified
loan book, is considered a good banking franchise and the share sale is expected to attract
significant market interest. The privatization process is expected to be completed by mid 2002.



- 31 -
III. TURKEY'S PUBLIC SECTOR REFORM PROGRAM
A.    Introduction
69.   The Govenmment's public sector reforn program aims to underpin sustained fiscal
adjustment and create the conditions for transparent and effective government. In combination
with the financial sector reform program described in Section II, the public sector reform
program addresses the underlying structural factors that led to the crisis, thereby trying to ensure
that these conditions do not recur in the future. In particular, the program aims to break the
vicious circle of inadequate public sector management leading to ever increasing public
indebtedness that fuels financial sector weakness through dependency on high return government
securities which in turn inflates the public debt burden.
70.   The program   focuses on three critical areas, each of which has a medium-term
dimension:
*  Implement structural fiscal policies to ensure permanent fiscal adjustment;
*  Launch a medium-term program of policy and institutional reforms to improve the
transparency and efficiency of public expenditure management (PEM) including action
to:
*  Improve budget preparation and execution, policy formulation, and the operational
performance of public agencies,
*  Upgrade public accounting, procurement and audit standards to ensure financial
accountability, and
* Ensure prudent public liability management; and
* Initiate broad based institutional reforns to improve the quality of public sector
governance.
71.   The underlying problems with the structure and management of the public sector have
been analyzed in detail in the CEM on Structural Reforms for Sustainable Growth and the Public
Expenditure and Institutional Review. Table 6 shows that Turkey's public sector is large relative
to OECD comparators such as Spain and Portugal, and that public spending has greatly exceeded
available resources." Countries like Germany and Italy, with comparably high levels of
expenditure sustain this with much greater revenue effort and therefore run much smaller
deficits. Central to this problem is extensive Govemment intervention throughout the economy
and an under performing system of public sector management. The first dimension of the
problem is being addressed though the on-going structural reforms to the social security system,
to modernize agriculture support policies, deregulate energy and telecommunications, and
implement privatization. The second dimension arises from the long neglect of the need to
modernize the procedures and institutions which govern decision-making and resource allocation
II Including a monetary correction for nominal interest payments would reduce the general government deficit for
Turkey in Table 6 to about 3 percent of GDP in 1999. However, this does not include duty losses of the state banks
(estimated at about 6 percent of GDP in 1999 after monetary correction) or the losses of the SEE sector. The
operational deficit for the public sector as a whole is estimated to have exceeded 12 percent of GDP in 1999.

in the public sector. The impact of many years of weak fiscal discipline and poor expenditure
management is also reflected in the composition of public expenditure. The share of public
spending on interest payments and wages and salaries in Turkey varies greatly from almost all of
its OECD counterparts. This difference is inconsistent with the objectives of sustained economic
growth and equity.
B.      Recent Developments in Public Sector Reform
72.     The Government has made substantial progress in defining and implementing its public
sector reform program since last July. Two of the four public sector reform triggers for PFPSAL
II as set out in the Memorandum      of the President for the first PFPSAL (Box 3) have been fully
met. These cover rationalization of the public investment program and enactment of the new
public procurement law. The public debt management law is expected to be adopted by
Parliament by end-March as a condition of Board presentation of PFPSAL 11 and enacted shortly
thereafter, thereby meeting the third trigger. The fourth trigger concerning satisfactory progress
in consolidating the budget to improve its scope and transparency will be met prior to the release
of the third tranche of the loan. These policy and institutional reforms are core components of
the Government's strategy for improving public expenditure management in Turkey. In the area

of structural fiscal policies, the Government has: (i) adopted a medium-term strategy for

sector. The latter program is a cornerstone of the Government's efforts to modernize the public
sector. In the area of public sector governance, the Government has adopted a national strategy
for improving governance and combating corruption which will be published by end-March.
73.     The implementation of the new legislation on public procurement and debt management,
together the other elements of the Government's public sector reform agenda-including the tax
strategy, public employment program and anti-corruption strategy, will be supported by the
second and third tranches of the PFPSAL II, as well as the proposed PPSAL and the expected
follow-up PPSAL II under the next CAS. To coordinate implementation of this complex agenda,
the Government intends to establish a new structure involving a small group of key ministers
acting as a Steering Group with clear coordination and program implementation responsibilities.
Under the new structure, the steering committee for public expenditure management reform will
operate under the authority of this Group. A committee will also be formed under the Steering
Group to support implementation of the national anti-corruption strategy. The ministerial



- 34 -
committee for civil service reform, which has already been established, will work in close
coordination with the new Steering Group. More detailed information on the status of the public
sector reform program and expected follow-up actions is presented in the paragraphs below.
(C.         ncrCudnrlR Fscal Policies
74.     The Government is implementing additional fiscal measures to meet the primary surplus
target of 6.5 percent of GNP for the consolidated public sector-of which 5.4 percent of GNP for
the central government--set in the budget for 2002 (Table 7). While the primary surplus is
targeted to increase by one percent of GNP in 2002, the authorities have identified fiscal
measures equivalent to two percent of GNP in order to compensate for certain revenue
weaknesses and expenditure pressures (Box 4). In particular, tax revenue on interest income is
projected to fall from 3.6 percent of GNP in 2001 to about 2.4 percent in 2002 as interest rates
ease. In response, the authorities are increasing excise taxes, notably those on petroleum
products, in some cases with significant negative externalities and distortions. A broader, more
comprehensive effort is needed to shift revenues to a more sustainable base as envisaged in the
medium-term tax strategy discussed below (para. 77).

Box 4: Fiscal Package for 2002
Revenue Increases (1.2 percent of GNP)
*   Petroleum consumption tax (PCT) was increased by 6 percent in November 2001 (diesel by 35 percent),
thereafter the PCT will continue to be increased at a rate at least equal to WPI inflation; a PCT on natural gas has
been introduced, effective May 2002, at a specific rate of TL 5,000 per cubic meter to be subsequently indexed
to the WPI.
*   Special transactions tax was increased by 50 percent and the motor vehicles tax by 75 percent, effective January
1, 2002.
*   The property tax rate was increased by 100 percent: and the share of tax revenues allocated to metropolitan
municipalities reduced from 5 percent to 4.1 percent (revenue neutral for the municipalities).
*   Administered prices were increased in November 2001 as follows: alcohol and tobacco products by 25 percent,
Turk Telekom tariffs by 12 percent, and sugar by 25 percent. Sugar prices were increased by another 15 percent
in December 2001. Electricity tariffs were restored by November 2001 to US 4.5 cents benchmark set under
ERL program, after falling in mid-2001 as a result of the devaluation. Discounted tariffs by SEEs were
eliminated in January 2002.
*   Legal and regulatory measures were implemented in December 2001 to improve collections of electricity bills
by TEDAS.
Personnel and Benefit Expenditures (0.5 percent of GNP)
*   Maintain civil service salaries constant in real terms according to the current indexation system and maintain the
ceiling on the total number of civil servants in the consolidated budget (excluding transferred state bank civil
servants); the replacement hiring ratio for the government budget agencies except education, health and security
will be reduced from 80 percent to 50 percent effective January 2002; rents for civil servants living in state
housing were increased by 50 percent in December 2001.
*   Limit on replacement hiring by SEEs in 2002 was tightened from 15 percent to 10 percent of attrition and to 0
percent for SEEs financed from the consolidated budget; mandatory retirement for public sector workers over 50
years of age was authorized in December 2001; measures have been taken to reduce the wage bill in SEEs by 10
percent relative to the total projected wage bill (postponement of two bonus payments until 2003 can be
authorized by the Council of Ministers).
*   Reduce regulated profit margins for medicine, tighten quality controls, limnit prescription to necessary amounts,
withhold a 10 percent co-payment for prescriptions for Emekli Sandigi and Bag-Kur pensioners; legislation
passed in January 2002 to increase health premia for Bag-Kur members by 5 percentage points (broader
legislation to reform Bag-Kur administration remains pending in Parliament).
Operating Expenditures (0.2 percent of GNP)
*   Eliminate regional level administration and other regional line agency offices (legislation pending); gradually
shift the workers, equipment and responsibilities of certain departments to the provincial level administrations
(legislation pending). Transfers to these departments will be reduced by TL :330 trillion; efficiency gains and
additional revenues associated with revaluing properties for tax purposes (expected in 2002) should enable local
government to cope with this burden.
*   Instruct SEEs to lower general administration expenses and reduce inventories; eliminate loss-making railroad
routes and unprofitable THY flights; and close idle airports.
Agriculture Subsidies (0.1 percent of GNP)
*   Reduce indirect agriculture subsidies and budgetary transfers to SEKER in line with the increase in sugar prices.
75.    While the fiscal package for 2002 continues to rely heavily on revenue measures, it does
include a number of significant expenditure measures as well. Most importantly, the package
incorporates an initial set of policies to promote retrenchment of public employment (primarily



- 36 -
in the SEE sector) discussed in more detail below (para. 81) as part of the longer-term process to
modernize the public sector. Also reflected in the fiscal package is the continued progress in
reforming agriculture support policies including the termination of the agriculture credit
subsidies temporarily reintroduced in 2001, the reduction of indirect subsidies for specific
products and cuts in budgetary transfers to SEKER, the state sugar company. 12 These measures
will increase the funding available for the DIS program.
76.    Most of the elements of the 2002 fiscal package have been introduced by the Government
as a condition for Board presentation of PFPSAL II and as a prior action for the new IMF Stand-
by Arrangement. Implementation of the 2002 fiscal package, including implementation of the
remaining measures, will be monitored during PFPSAL II supervision in close collaboration with
the IMF. The Government is committed to take additional action in the context of the 2002
fiscal package, if needed to meet the primary surplus target. While the 2002 fiscal package
incorporates a number of important structural measures, it continues a pattern of reliance on
revenue increases over expenditure cuts and on temporary measures. Moreover, a number of
provisions in the package are likely to prove difficult to enforce in practice, particularly steps to
reduce operational expenditures. Over the medium term, deeper and more permanent structural
fiscal measures will be needed in order to sustain the fiscal adjustment. These structural
measures will complete and build on the results achieved under the ERL program particularly in
the areas of social security reform, energy reform, and reform of agriculture support policies.
77.    The Government has adopted a medium-term strategy for improving the tax system in
Turkey based on a review carried out jointly with the World Bank. The overarching objective of
the strategy is to improve the stability, transparency and equity of the tax system through
measures to minimize tax distortions, broaden the tax base and improve the efficiency of tax
administration. This will allow an equivalent amount of revenue to be raised at lower marginal
tax rates thereby promoting economic growth. The strategy builds on the 1998 tax reform and
draws on earlier analysis of the tax system including work carried out under the Bank's Public
Financial Management Project (PFMP). It does not purport to be a full-fledged tax reform at this
stage.  Studies of the tax system  by the IMF and World Bank13 and of the investment
environment by FIAS14 have signaled concerns about the complexity and lack of transparency of
Turkey's tax system, which is exacerbated by tax policy instability coupled with high and
unstable inflation rates. Much of the complexity and instability arises from the proliferation of
special and additional taxes and surcharges as a result of urgent pressures to reduce the fiscal
12 The 2002 budget does include TL 50 trillion introduced by the Parliament to fund credit subsidies for smnall
business through Halk Bank. This will be funded by increased transfers to the budget from the operational surpluses
of the Istanbul Stock Exchange, Capital Markets Board and other regulatory agencies.
13 Liam Embrill, Victoria Summers et al, Turkey: The Agenda for Tax Reform - The Next Steps, IME, Fiscal Affairs
Department (September 2000), and Sheetal K. Chand, David Robinson and Jose da Silva-Lopes, Turkey: Reform of
the Financial Sector Taxation, IMF (March 1990). Ismail Arslan and Sweder van Wijnbergen, The Investment and
Export Incentive System in Turkey, World Bank, December 1995.
14 Foreign Investment Advisory Service, Turkey: A Diagnostic Study of the Foreign Direct Investment Environment,
International Finance Corporation and World Bank, February 2001, and FIAS, Turkey: Administrative Barriers to
Investment, (June 2001).

Box 5: Tax Strategy Priorities
Tax Policy
*   Simplify and consolidate the indirect tax structure with a VAT with a standard rate and two low rates (1
percent for agriculture and 8 percent for other basic consumption goods). Introduce a unified Special
Consumption Tax that will consolidate the current range of excise and specific taxes into a single tax charged
on a limited range of luxury goods. A threshold will be established for obligatory filing of VAT returns.
Earmarking of revenues will be abolished and all revenues from earmarked taxes will be brought into the
budget.
*   Simplify and consolidate the direct tax regime in line with OECD standards and international best practice.
Convert tax rebate for wage earners into a tax credit to simply administration and reduce tax burden on low
income wage earners. Minimize tax exemptions and exceptions.
*   Harmonize real effective tax rates across all types of financial instruments and maturities.
*   Phase in the full indexation of the corporate and business income taxes (real interest deduction to replace
nominal interest deduction). Reduce the scope of investment incentives, consolidate into a single investment
allowance rate across sectors and geographic regions, and gradually eliminate withholding tax on these
allowances. Make the incentive system more transparent and automatic.
*   Overhaul the tax regime for free trade zones. Terminate the corporate tax and payroll tax exemptions.
Provide reduced corporate tax rate only on condition that separate subsidiaries are established and inward
trading is below 15 percent of sales.
* Adjust taxation of financial leasing in line with internationally generally accepted accounting practices.
Tax Admninistration
*   Reorganize the GDR to introduce functional structure in line with OECD practice.
*   Revise payment scheme for managers and key technical staff and design a bonus and incentive system which
allows more competitive total remuneration for these positions.
*   Upgrade the tax policy department within the GDR.
*   Strengthen tax audit management capacity within the MOF by establishing an audit coordination unit and
upgrade the role of the audit department in the GDR. Prepare a coordinated annual auditing plan. Develop
databases for tax audit planning and the use of information from the tax identification number (TIN) system
for effective auditing. Design a more comprehensive large taxpayer audit strategy and improve knowledge
and database on large taxpayers and their business transactions.
*   Develop a strategy for taxpayer services and improvements in relations between taxpayers and the GDR.
*   Upgrade internal audit function for the tax administration reporting directly to the head of GDR.
*   Design and implement program to reduce tax arrears.
*   Upgrade the management information systems throughout the GDR.
deficit. Partial inflation indexation in combination with differential nominal tax rates and
investment incentives across financial instruments have left highly distorted real effective tax
rates across financial instruments and real business investments. Investors have identified
bureaucratic red tape, delays in processing applications for licenses or investment allowances
and VAT refund claims, and corruption as additional negative factors affecting investment and
business operations.
78.    The Government's tax strategy lays out a series of coordinated actions over the course of
the next three years to address certain serious tax policy and administration issues facing Turkey.
The priority objectives are summarized in Box 5. The details and sequencing of specific actions
are being discussed and agreed with the Bank and 1MF. In the area of tax policy, near-term
actions under the strategy are grouped into two phases to be implemented over the course of
2002. The first phase, to be enacted in a revenue-neutral manner by April 2002, focuses on



- 38 -
simplifying the system of indirect taxation. The second phase addresses improvements to the
system of direct taxation. The objectives are to: (i) harmonize taxes on investment income; (ii)
rationalize ad hoc inflation adjustments in the tax system and lessen distortions associated with
the taxation of nominal interest income; (iii) rationalize the system of investment incentives; and
(iv) overhaul the system of credits against income tax. Legislation for the direct tax changes will
be submitted to Parliament by October 2002 to take effect as of January 2003.
79.   The strategy also tackles tax administration issues encompassing institutional
improvements, automation, transparency, compliance, taxpayer services and tax audit. It is
designed to align Turkey progressively with best practice in other OECD countries. As a key
milestone, the MOF will publish a circular implementing the new functional structure within the
General Department of Revenues by June 2002. The functional reorganization will reorient the
GDR from a structure based on administration of individual taxes to one based on the core
functions of tax administration including taxpayer registration, taxpayer services, collections,
audit, legal, information technology, and human resources. The tax policy unit of GDR will be
upgraded. The MOF will also create an audit coordination committee at the ministerial level
which will be responsible for preparing annual audit plan. The audit capacity of GDR will also
be enhanced with additional resources. The legal framework for other priority actions in the area
of tax administration is expected to be in place before the end of 2002. Adoption of the tax
strategy was a condition of Board presentation for PFPSAL II. Satisfactorv implementation of
the strategv will be a condition for release of the second and third tranches of the loan. Further
progress in implementing the strategy to improve the tax system will be a benchmark for the
proposed PPSAL.
80.   Deeper structural measures are underway on the expenditure side as well, as part of a
longer-term strategy to modernize the public sector, ensure medium-term fiscal sustainability,
and free up resources for private investment and job creation in support of Turkey's growth
objectives. This strategy includes action to: (i) withdraw the state from direct involvement in
productive activities through the restructuring and privatization of SEEs and reform of
agriculture support policies, (ii) contain and then gradually eliminate the deficits of the social
security institutions, (iii) curtail the scope of the government's contingent liabilities through the
introduction of an energy market and transparent guidelines for financial and project guarantees
under the new public debt law, (iv) restructure central government agencies and decentralize
additional functions to provincial and local governments in coordination with efforts to improve
governance and financial accountability at the local level, and (v) implement a comprehensive
public employment program, including retrenchment of SEE employment. The retrenchment in
SEE employment supports the policy of a non-inflationary, sustained shift in the overall SEE
balance from deficit to surplus (see Table 7). While reducing SEE employment is always
difficult and entails social costs, the fiscal adjustment it supports will reduce crowding out and
promote the strategically important objective of private sector investment and job creation,
particularly for Turkey's youth. Under the public employment program, the Government
successfully met its objective of holding stable the number of civil servants under the
consolidated budget in 2001, while ensuring additional recruitment to meet needs in the social
sectors, notably education. The policy of replacing up to a maximum of 15 percent of retiring
personnel in the SEEs led to a modest reduction in their total workforce in 2001 (Table 8).



- 39 -
81.   Going forward, the Government is taking a more comprehensive approach to public
employmnent policy. For 2002, the focus of the program is on retrenchment of redundant labor in
the SEE sector and consolidation of aggregate limits on civil service employment in advance of
deeper civil service reform. The ceiling on civil service employment in the central govemment
agencies is being renewed for 2002 and expanded to cover civil servants employed in the local
administrations.15 The limit on replacement hiring in the SEEs has been tightened to 10 percent
of attrition and no replacement hiring will be authorized for SEEs requiring finance from the
budget. In addition, the hiring of security personnel has been included in the SEE replacement
hiring rule for 2002. In December 2001, long-standing restrictions were lifted on the ability of
managers to require public sector workers over 50 years of age to retire. Approximately 15,000
public sector workers were retired or notified of their retirement, laid off, or converted to private
contract status between December 2001 and end-January 2002. The large majority of these
public sector workers were employed in the SEEs. Civil service employment is regulated by
specific labor legislation and civil servants are not subject to this early retirement policy.
82.   The Government has identified (with Bank assistance under the PSSP project) redundant
public sector workers in Turk Telekom and in the PA portfolio of companies. The Government
is updating and expanding this earlier analysis to identify all redundant workers and positions in
SEEs. As of end-January, the Government had eliminated 90 percent of all unfilled SEE
positions. By end-April, the Government will translate the updated information into specific
positions in the companies, and determine how to meet the target of reducing the identified
redundancies by one third by end-June through voluntary retirement offers and layoffs only
when necessary.  Using company-specific information, the Government will by end-May
produce final estimates of redundant positions. These redundancies will be reduced by a
cumulative two thirds by end-October 2002, and, by end-June 2003, the remaining redundancies
will be phased out. For this well-targeted attrition, no replacement hiring will be allowed, and
the resulting unfilled positions will be immediately eliminated. Compliance with this program
by individual SEEs will be audited on a quarterly basis. The Government is also undertaking
coordinated efforts to ensure that adequate finding is in place for the lump-sum severance
payments due to retiring and laid-off workers.16 This is particularly important for loss-making
SEEs which cannot cover these payments from their own cash flow. 7 The net cost of this
initiative (severance payments minus wage savings) will not be counted towards the primary
surplus target for 2002 up to a limit of TL 1.25 quadrillion.
83.   To ensure close coordination among the relevant agencies and the necessary political will
for these tough measures, the Government has confided responsibility for the public employment
program to a newly established ministerial committee which will also be responsible for civil
service reform (see para. 105 below). Initiation of the comprehensive public employment
15 In practice, new civil service hiring in the local administrations will be limited to 2001 attrition.
16 With the introduction of the new unemployment insurance scheme, the Bank has recommended to the authorities
to consider a fundamental restructuring of the severance payment system over the medium term.
17 Experience to date under the PSSP project provides a rough upper bound for severance payment costs of some
US$15,000 per worker. Additional costs arising from reduced social security contributions have been factored into
the 2002 fiscal programL

program was a Board condition for PFPSAL II and its further implementation will be a condition
