will be supported by the envisaged PPSAL.
D.         Public Expenditure Management
84.        The key elements of the Government's multi-year strategy for public expenditure
management (PEM) reform are laid out in its Strategic Framework for Public Expenditure
Management Reform. This strategic framework is the result of many years of internal discussion
in Turkey about the need to modernize public expenditure management, particularly at the higher
levels of the government bureaucracy. It draws on the report of the Ad Hoc Committee on
restructuring of public financial management and fiscal transparency which was subsequently
reflected in the eighth 5 year Development Plan. It also draws extensively on the PEIR and
incorporates recommendations from the Fiscal Transparency Review carried out by the IMF in
2000. The Government's strategic framework was annexed to the LDP for the first PFPSAL and



- 41 -
remains the basis for the PEM reforns under the PFPSAL program and subsequent
programmatic operations. The framework is structured in matrix fashion into three priority areas
in support of three core objectives. The objectives, commonly referred to in international
practice as levels 1-3, are: strengthening aggregate discipline (level 1), building capacity for
policy formulation (level 2), and improving the performance of public agencies (level 3). The
three priorities for PEM reform are to:
*  Reform the processes for budget preparation and execution. This involves steps to: (i)
improve the transparency and comprehensiveness of the budget in line with international
standards, (ii) strengthen the credibility of the budget preparation process, (iii) build capacity
for policy formulation at all levels of government, and (iv) realize concrete improvements in
the operational performance of line ministries and agencies through a progressive shift to
perfornance-oriented budgeting.
*  Upgrade public accounting, procurement and audit functions to ensure adeguate financial
accountability. This encompasses legal changes to introduce international fiduciary
standards, as well as institutional changes to build capacity to implement the new standards
and shift from formalistic ex ante control to effective ex post monitoring.
*  Ensure prudent public liability management. This involves legal measures to establish clear
lines of borrowing authority and transparent reporting of public liabilities, as well as
institutional measures to build up the capacity for modem fiscal risk management.
85.    Budget Reform. The Government continues to make significant progress in improving
the transparency and scope of the budget. In the 2002 budget, all budgetary and extra-budgetary
funds have been closed with the exception of the Support Price Stabilization Fund (DFIF), linked
to the restructuring of the agriculture sales cooperative unions (ASCUs) under the ARIP, and
five EBFs. The Government has renewed its commitment not to create new budgetary or extra-
budgetary funds. The DFIF will be closed at the end of the Agriculture Reform Implementation
Project with the World Bank. For the remaining EBFs (excluding the SDIF which is covered
under the financial sector reform program), the Government will undertake the following actions
with effect from the 2003 budget: (i) submit their budgets to Parliament for approval, (ii) audit
their accounts annually, and (iii) report their operations monthly on a consolidated basis with the
central government. The presentation of the 2002 budget to the Parliament was supplemented
with the submission in October of the accounts and financial outlook for the contingent liabilities
of the Treasury, the remaining extra budgetary funds, the social security institutions, autonomous
agencies, local authorities", and SEEs.'9 The number of revolving funds which are used by local
institutions to supplement budget allocations was reduced from over 2,600 to 1,981 by late 2001,
and a further reduction to about 1,400 is expected by end-March 2002. In implementing this
rationalization, the Government has to pay particular attention not to degrade the operational
perfornance of agencies which rely on revolving funds.
18 There are three levels of local authority in Turkey: (i) 80 Special Provincial Administrations are the central
government's agencies at regional level, (ii) about 3,300 municipalities have been established in settlements of more
than 2,000 inhabitants (and in provincial and district centres regardless of population size), and are home to about
70% of Turkey's population, and (iii) some 35,000 villages governed by elected head-men (Muhtar) and council-of-
elders. Local authorities have accounted for some 4.5 percent of GNP in public expenditure in recent years.
3 9This information was presented in the Public Finance Report prepared by SPO in September 2001.

86.   In June 2001, the Government completed a new budget classification for consolidated
budget agencies in line with new GFS standards. All preparations have been completed for
implementation of the new GFS classification on a pilot basis in the 2002 budget. Six pilot
agencies have been selected (Table 9) and training has been carried out for their staff. Three of
these agencies will participate in a joint pilot of the new budget classification and accounting
standards (para. 94). An article has been included in the 2002 annual budget law to give the
MOF authority to expand the new budget classification to cover all of general government as
defined by GFS.20 The above actions meet the conditions for Board presentation of the PFPSAL
II. As a condition for release of the second tranche of the loan, the MOF will issue a circular
expanding the definition of the GFS budget classification to all general government agencies by
March 2002. Satisfactory implementation of the pilot GFS program will be a condition for
release of the third tranche of the loan. Rollout of the new budget classification for the
consolidated budget agencies will begin with the 2003 budget cycle. The new GFS budget
classification is expected to be fully operational for the entire general govermment in the 2004
budget.

87.   Important steps have been taken to improve the credibility of the budget preparation
process and rationalize the public investment program (PIP) in the 2002 budget cycle. The
framework for these actions was established under the first PFPSAL by a High Planning Council
decision issued in June 2001 to accompany the Prime Minister's Budget Call. The HPC decision
provided a macro-fiscal framework for 2002 budget preparation including overall levels for the
recurrent and investment budget, (ii) established indicative ceilings for both the recurrent and
investment budgets for ministries and line agencies based on an indexation formula applied to
the actual budget allocations that each ministry and line agency received in 2001, and (iii) called
for a rationalization of the PIP in the 2002 budget with the objective of reducing expected
average time to completion of the overall PIP by 20 percent in 2002 along with limits on the
introduction of new multi-year investment projects except for emergency projects. While the
2002 budget preparation process remained far from perfect, the broad framework provided by the
HPC did serve to guide decision making particularly at the center. The full cooperation of the

88.    In January 2002, the SPO     published the PIP for 2002 which is in line with the
rationalization criteria set by the High Planning Council (Table 10). As part of the PIP
rationalization, a Public Investment Review was carried out on an urgent basis by SPO in
October 2001 with support from the Bank. The PIR confirmed that the average completion time
is excessively long, reflecting a lack of prioritization among projects. In 2001, the average
completion time was 12.5 years21 (more than 30 years in the agriculture sector), a sharp increase
from 9.2 years in 2000 given the impact of the Lira devaluation and higher than expected
inflation. Scarce funds are distributed among an excessive number of projects which leads to
inefficiencies in project implementation. The PIP has traditionally included projects that will not
be implemented immediately (the so called "trace allocations"), which hampers the credibility of
the program. In response to the HPC's decision to rationalize the investment program, the 2002
PIP cuts the projected average completion time to 8.5 years, a 32 percent reduction which
significantly exceeds the 20 percent benchmark set by the HPC. The 2002 PIP: (i) phases large
projects with the view to focusing on priority subprojects; (ii) excludes from the investment
program numerous projects with "trace allocation"; (iii) terminates a large number of
uneconomic projects; and (iv) reduces by more than half the number of new multi-year projects
21 The 12.5 years average completion time is calculated by SPO based on updated average exchange rate and
inflation estimates for 2001 (TL 1.23 mnillion per US Dollar and a deflator of 60 percent). The average completion
time for 2001 was originally projected to be 9.4 years on the basis of the TL 0.714 mnillion per US Dollar average
exchange rate and 18 percent deflator in the May program.



-44 -
included in the 2002 investment program. Most of the new multi-year projects are emergency
projects (e.g. flood prevention) or projects of a social nature.22 Overall, 353 individual projects
and 649 subprojects have been dropped from the PIP and the rationalization has reduced the total
cost of the PIP by TL 36.9 quadrillion. Publication of the rationalized 2002 PIP meets the
relevant condition for Board presentation of PFPSAL II. The Government intends to adopt by
March an action plan, as a condition for release of the second tranche of the loan, which will lead
to further improvements in the public investment program over the 2003-04 budget cycles. The
Bank has recommended that the action plan include a target for further reduction in the average
project completion time over the next two years and continued limits on the introduction of new
multi-year projects, as well as steps to strengthen the role of formal cost/benefit analysis in the
project approval process and establish a framework for project monitoring and evaluation.
Implementation of this action plan in the 2003-04 budget cycles would be supported by the
PPSAL and possible follow-up PPSAL II.
89.   Budget reforms are also underway to build capacity for policy formulation and improve
the performance of public agencies. The SPO has prepared draft guidelines for strategic
planning by key line ministries and departments including guidelines for costing policies. The
guidelines for strategic planning are under review and will be finalized prior to release of the
second tranche of PFPSAL II. The Government will also initiate policy reviews by selected line
ministries and agencies to derive clear statement of objectives and expenditure priorities in
preparation of 2003 budget. This will be complemented by action to revive the role of
ministerial departments responsible for policy reviews with support from SPO. These additional
actions are expected to be carried out prior to release of the third tranche of PFPSAL II.
90.   The Government is carrying out a pilot program in 2002 for a group of line ministries and
agencies willing to volunteer for reforms that will provide them with greater discretionary
control over inputs linked to adoption of effective intemal controls and commitment to improved
performance. Six agencies for the pilot programn in 2002 have been identified (Table 11). The
pilot program will start with a limited set of programs within these agencies and is expected to be
gradually expanded over time. The legal basis for providing block appropriations to these
agencies has been included in the 2002 budget law. Inplementation of the pilot program will be
monitored during supervision of PFPSAL II and the Bank will provide close support to the
Government to carry out the program effectively.
91.   The Government intends to undertake further budget reforms during the 2002-04 period.
Completion of the GFS pilot program and rollout of the GFS budget classification to all of the
consolidated budget agencies in the 2003 budget cycle would be supported by the PPSAL. The
PPSAL would support additional enhancements to the preparation process for the 2003 budget-
including introduction of a rolling two-year expenditure framework, along with further efforts to
build the capacity for policy formulation and implementation of the pilot program to improve
operational performance and its expansion beyond the initial set of agencies and programns. The
22 There are however two new large transport projects (airport safety and railway electrification) which account for
about half of the TL 2.25 quadrillion total cost of new multi-year projects. The railway project will be implemented
only if adequate external financing can be found.

core priorities of the budget reform-particularly the level 1 refornis in support of greater

92.   Financial Accountability.  Modernization of Turkey's system of public financial
accountability in line with international standards is an urgent PEM priority. As detailed in the
August 2001 CFAA, some of the basic building blocks appear to be in place: an established legal
framework; reliable, albeit fragmented, accounting processes; regular reporting of financial
results; an apparently independent Supreme Audit Institution, the Turkish Court of Accounts
(TCA), which reports to Parliament; a relatively open system of accountability and plenty of
skilled personnel. However, deeper analysis reveals significant problems and lack of systemic
coherence. Complex institutional relationships, multiple sources of public funds, heavy emphasis
on ex ante controls and inadequate reporting to the legislature reduce transparency and weaken
financial management. The accounting law which governs public sector accounting dates from
1927, and procurement legislation has not kept up with evolving international standards. Similar
to the budget, the audit system is fragmented with many bodies besides the TCA carrying out
activities analogous to external inspection and audit.
93.   Accounting. Efforts to upgrade the public accounting system are gaining momentum
under the PFPSAL program after many years of limited progress. The MOF has essentially
completed the installation and commissioning of the say2000i automated accounting system in
the approximately 1,500 sites of the General Directorate of Public Accounting across Turkey.
Under this internet based system, the MOF's accounting data base will be held centrally and will
contain data on all individual transactions wherever they occur in the system. The say2000i
system went live on January 1, 2002 for the consolidated budget agencies thereby meeting a key
benchmark for PFPSAL II. By June 2002, the Goverunent intends to complete three key
interfaces for the say2000i system: (i) to the budget system of the General Directorate of Budget
and Financial Control of the MOF, (ii) to the VEDOP revenue system of the General Directorate
of Revenues, and (iii) to the accountancies of the Treasury. This will provide a fully integrated,



-46 -
automated system for tracking central government revenue and expenditure in Turkey. By the
end of 2002, all accountancies will have abandoned the old system and moved exclusively to
say2000i. However, it is too early to assess the extent of potential problems that may occur
during the implementation phase, and sometime after mid-2002 a full-scale assessment of
say2000i will be needed. The extent to which say2000i is providing accurate and timely
information to managers will be monitored during PFPSAL II supervision.
94.   The authority of the MOF to set government accounting standards for all general
government agencies in line with GFS has been provided by the same article in the 2002 budget
law as for the budget classification. Accounting standards on the modified accrual basis,
manual, and chart of accounts consistent with GFS have been drafted and were issued to the
three agencies participating in the joint pilot program on January 23, 2002.23 These actions
satisfy the conditions for Board presentation of PFPSAL II. By end-March 2002, MOF will
issue the new draft national public accounting standards on the modified accruals basis to all
general government agencies in line with GFS. By June 2002. the new accounting standards and
statutory chart of accounts for the general govermment will be published in the official gazette as
24
a condition for release of the third tranche of the loan.  In parallel, a new Law on Public
Financial Management and Financial Control (PFMFC) is being prepared to replace the existing
public accounting law (No. 1050). This legislation will make permanent the MOF's authority to
set budget coding and accounting standards for the general government. More broadly, it will
modernize the legal framework for public accounting and financial control in line with
international standards, and clear the way for the phased introduction of full accrual accounting
over the longer term. In this regard, the new law will go beyond the public accounting
benchmarks set for PFPSAL II. The Government intends to submit the PFMFC legislation to
Parliament by end-June 2002 as a condition for release of the third tranche of PFPSAL II.
95.   Deepening of the public accounting reform will be supported by the PPSAL and possible
PPSAL II in 2003-04. Enactment of the PFMFC Law, expected by late 2002, will be a trigger
for the PPSAL. In the 2003 budget cycle, the GFS framework will be rolled out to all
consolidated budget agencies and the pilot will shift to the remaining agencies of the general
government. This timetable will provide adequate time for the extensive training required,
particularly for local administrations. The new GFS framework for modified accrual accounting
is scheduled to be fully in place to support the rollout of the new budget classification to all of
general government in the 2004 budget cycle. The Ministry of Finance is also developing a
strategy for phased introduction of full accrual accounting across general govermment agencies.
However, the Bank and the Government have agreed that adoption of this strategy, originally a
benchmark for PFPSAL II, should be shifted to the 2004 budget cycle (i.e., to the possible
PPSAL II operation) given other pressing priority actions under the accounting reform.
23 The new chart of accounts is based on an economic classification. The coding for the functional, institutional and
financial classifications are being built into the accounting software running under say2000i.
24 This will be a framework chart of accounts which will be adapted by MOF to meet the specific requirements of
individual agencies while maintaining overall consistency with GFS standards.



- 47 -
96.   Procurement. Turkey has moved decisively over the past six months to upgrade its
public procurement legislation and practices in line with international standards. The new public
procurement law was enacted on January 22, 2002 thereby meeting a key condition for Board
presentation of PFPSAL II. The new law is based on the UNICTRAL model and moves Turkey
towards compliance with EU requirements. It was prepared in close collaboration with World
Bank and EU experts, and incorporates the key recommendations of the CPAR. The law covers
all goods, works, and services-with the exception of military contracts-and encompasses both
budgetary and non-budgetary procurement. It is applicable to central and local government
agencies, as well as SEEs. The new law stipulates the qualification requirements (including
technical and financial criteria) for contractors which will supercede the existing contractor
certificate system. The law establishes thresholds (in TL) above which foreign bidders cannot be
restricted and a domestic preference of 15 percent can be applied. However, the thresholds have
been set at high levels which could reduce competition and subject an excessive amount of
public procurement to shortened minimum time periods for bids.25 The Government intends to
enact amendments to the law by end-May 2002 to postpone indexation of the thresholds until
2004, which will have the effect of lowering their real value, and to increase the amount of
procurement subject to the full minimum bidding periods stipulated in the law. Consistent with
international best practice, the law creates an independent agency to oversee public procurement
in Turkey and ensure enforcement of the new procurement standards. The new public
procurement agency is expected to be established and its Board of Directors appointed by end-
March 2002. Enactment of the agreed amendments to the law and establishment of the public
procurement agency will be conditions for release of the third tranche of the loan. The agency
will be responsible for preparing the required regulations and model contracts by the end of
2002. The new procurement standards will come into force in January 2003. The EU may
provide the new agency with technical assistance to draft the required secondary legislation.
Technical assistance may also be forthcoming from the Bank. Implementation of the new
legislation and related efforts to strengthen institutional capacity would be supported by the
PPSAL operation, together with steps to prepare for the introduction of periodic procurement
audits by TCA and publication of the results.
97.   Auditing.  Effective financial accountability requires extensive modernization of
Turkey's public audit system.  The objectives are two fold: (i) clarify institutional
responsibilities, promote improvements in audit quality in line with international standards and
support the shift from ex ante controls to ex post monitoring in harmony with the efforts to
improve operational performance, and (ii) expand the scope of TCA audits to cover the entire
general government including local administrations, autonomous agencies, social security
institutions, remaining EBFs and revolving funds, with the overall objective of transforming
TCA into an effective supreme audit institution. The first objective will be achieved through
enactment of the new law on Public Financial Management and Financial Control. This law will
be the cornerstone for modernization of both public accounting and public audit functions in line
with international best practice and EU directives. On the audit side, the law will clarify the
25 At the current exchange rate, the thresholds are approximately equivalent to: US$535,000 for procurement of
goods and services by central government agencies, US$715,000 for procurement of goods and services by sub-
national entities, and US$12.5 million for procurement of works.



-48 -
roles and responsibilities of the line agencies, the MOF and the TCA. In tandem with parallel
changes in the TCA law, it will allow TCA to discontinue involvement in budget execution and
focus on ex-post audits, including performance audits. As was the case with the public
procurement law, the Bank is closely coordinating with the EU its advice on the draft PFMFC
legislation. In February, a discussion draft of the new PFMFC law was submitted by MOF to the
audit task force established in June 2001 under the PEM Steering Committee to coordinate the
work on audit reform. The draft is being reviewed by the task force, and then it will be
circulated to all line ministries and TCA. As mentioned above (para. 93), the PFMFC law will
be submitted to Parliament by end-June 2002 prior to release of the third tranche of PFPSAL II.
98.   The agenda for expanding the scope of TCA audits centers on the following three actions:
* Include all consolidated budget agencies under TCA's annual audit as required by the
Constitution, including the Presidency and Parliament.
* Subject TCA's own accounts to external audit with reports submitted to the Parliament.
* Adopt a plan of action to expand scope of TCA audits to cover the entire general government
in line with the objective of establishing an effective supreme audit institution.
99.   Action on this agenda has been delayed by the need for Parliament to appoint a new TCA
president, now expected by end-March 2002. As a condition of Board presentation for PFPSAL
II. draft legislation to include the Presidency and Parliament under TCA's annual audit and to
subject TCA's own accounts to external audit will be submitted to Parliament by end-March
2002. Enactment of this legislation and implementation of the proposal for external audit of
TCA are expected by April 2002 prior to release of the second tranche of the loan. Adoption of
the action plan to expand TCA audits to all of general government is expected prior to release of
the third tranche of the loan. Building consensus on the action plan among the institutions
concerned, particularly those whose own audit mandates will be affected, will be critical to its
success. The plan will be complemented by capacity building measures designed to transform
TCA in line with the requirements for a modern, systems based audit institution. Ensuring
uniform and transparent accountability of Turkey's independent regulatory boards is an
important priority for both the audit reform and the credibility of the overall reform program. In
this context, the Government intends that: (i) by June 2002, the current legislation will be
amended to include TCA in the audit committee for each regulatory board in Turkey, and (ii) the
action plan for expanding the scope of TCA audits to all of general govermnent will include
measures to establish a new medium-term audit structure for the regulatory boards in line with
international best practice and the overall objective of establishing TCA as the supreme audit
institution in Turkey. Implementation of the PFMFC law and the action plan to expand the
scope of TCA audits, as well as the shift to ex-post financial and performance audits, would be
supported by the PPSAL and possible follow up PPSAL II in 2003-04.
100. Public Liability Management. Efforts to improve public liability management under
the PEM reform strategy focus on strengthening debt management and containing the spread of
contingent liabilities. These actions complement the energy reforms supported by the ERL that
are tackling a key source of contingent liabilities in the form of government guaranteed BOT
contracts, as well as the banking reforms supported by the PFPSAL that address the buildup of
quasi-fiscal and contingent liabilities in the banking system which triggered the February crisis.
Measures to strengthen public liability management will address other important sources of



-49 -
contingent liabilities and quasi-fiscal losses, including those arising from take-or-pay contracts
for natural gas imports, non-energy BOT projects, and guarantees to municipal and local
governments. Table 12 presents information on the guarantee portfolio of the Treasury.

101. The new law on Public Finance and Debt Management, expected to be adopted by
Parliament by end-March, will create the foundation for a comprehensive risk management
framework in Turkey. The new law will confirm the Treasury as the single borrowing authority
for the central govemment.    It will create a more transparent framework for public debt
management and the issuance of government guarantees. The new law will also establish a risk
account in the budget, starting with the 2003 budget, to cover the expected fiscal cost of called
guarantees. Adoption of the public debt law is a condition for Board presentation of PFPSAL II.



- 50 -
In accordance with the new law, the Government intends to adopt new regulations for the
issuance of government guarantees and for borrowing by SEEs by end-March 2002 as a
condition for release of the second tranche of the loan. As part of its program to improve public
liability management, the Treasury intends to strengthen further its risk management capacity by
establishing a strong middle office for risk management by June 2002 prior to release of the third
tranche of the loan. A review of the government guarantee portfolio and preparation of a debt
sustainability analysis, originally expected to be completed by end-2001, will now be completed
by December 2002. This work was delayed in part by the need to establish clearer lines of
responsibility for debt management within the Government and to agree with the Bank on terms
of reference for technical assistance.
102. As elements of the risk management framework, the Government plans over the course of
2002 to: (i) implement mechanisms for monitoring government project guarantees and on-
lending operations, (ii) develop a centralized fiscal risk analysis capability in the new middle
office, and (iii) prepare and disclose a Government Fiscal Risk Statement. These actions would
be supported by the PPSAL. The full institutional structure for the new public liability
management system, including all necessary legal changes, should be in place by the end of
2003. The Government will then be in a position to issue its public liability management strategy
as an annex to the planned medium-term expenditure framework.
E.    Public Sector Governance
103.  Actions to improve public sector governance aim to reduce political influence over
economic management and upgrade the quality and effectiveness of Turkey's public sector.
This agenda includes the preparation and implementation of a national anti-corruption strategy as
well as the launch of a comprehensive civil service reform. These actions complement and are
integrated with the programn of structural fiscal reform and PEM reform discussed earlier. A
national strategy to enhance transparency and good governance in the public sector was adopted
by the Govermment in January. The strategy was prepared by a steering committee under the
leadership of the Prime Minister's Inspection Board and including representatives from the
Treasury, the Ministry of Finance's Financial Crimes Investigation Board (MASAK), the
Ministry of Justice and the Ministry of Interior. The Government Decree adopting the strategy
and the Steering Committee's report under the slogan "A Transparent and Clean Turkey:
Together Hand in Hand" will be published by end-March. The objective of the strategy is to
provide a comprehensive framework for improving governance and reducing political influence
over the economy which establishes clear priorities and benchmarks, and empowers and
energizes public opinion to fight corruption.
104.  The strategy covers five core areas: public administration, the judicial system, the
political system, civil society and the competitive private sector. It complements on-going
regulatory reforms in the energy, telecommunications and financial sectors, as well as the
reforms to the public procurement system and the broader financial accountability agenda
discussed above. Priorities under the national anti-corruption strategy are summarized in Box 6
and the matrix of short to medium-term actions covering 2002-04 is presented in Annex VIII.
The Government is counting on the active involvement of national NGOs. One of the major
national NGOs, TESEV, has completed two diagnostic surveys on corruption covering

survey covering civil servants. These surveys have been co-sponsored by the World Bank. The
International Conference on Good Governance and Combating Corruption co-sponsored by the
Government and the Bank in September 2001 provided input on international experience and
best practice to help shape the strategy. Adoption and subsequent publication of the national
strategv to enhance transparency and good governance will fulfill a Board condition for PFPSAL
II. Effective launch of implementation of the strategy will be a condition for release of the
second tranche of the loan. The Government intends to put in place by April appropriate
arrangements to coordinate implementation of the anti-corruption strategy. The medium-term
elements of the anti-corruption strategy would be supported by the envisaged PPSAL operations.
105.   The Government realizes that sustained improvements in public govemance will hinge in
large part on effective reform   of the civil service.   Indeed, civil service reform   features
prominently in the national anti-corruption strategy. Civil service reform is vital to raising the
quality of public services and ensuring the quality of the fiscal adjustment. The Government has
already initiated work on a norm cadre system to establish benchmarks for staffing in public
agencies which is now operational within the Ministry of Education. However, there is
widespread acknowledgement that a more complete, medium-term approach is needed in order
to adapt the structure and function of the civil service to the changing role of government in
Turkey and to internalize the on-going reforms to public expenditure management. In addition
to questions about the size and composition of the civil service in a changing Turkey, the reform
will need to address issues such as distorted salary incentives, career development requirements



- 52 -
and inadequate operational budgets in most agencies. In February, as a condition of Board
presentation, the Government established a ministerial committee to oversee the preparation of a
civil service reform strategy and carry out a full functional review of government in preparation
for further decisions about what functions to retain within government and what to devolve to the
private sector. Recognizing the linkages between civil service reform and more immediate
actions to adjust public sector staffing levels, the Government has also confided oversight for the
public employment program to the same ministerial committee. Under the committee's
leadership, preparations for a functional review of government are expected to be launched by
March. Preparation of the civil service reform strategv will be fully underway as a condition for
release of the third tranche of the loan. The Government expects to adopt its strategy for civil
service reform in late 2002 as part of the program for PPSAL. Continued implementation of the
civil service reform program would be supported by the potential PPSAL II in the next CAS
period.



- 53 -
IV. THE PROPOSED LOAN
A.    The Bank's Financial Sector Assistance Strategy
106.  Background. Turkey and the Bank have had a long relationship in the financial sector
since the early 1980s through a series of SALs (I-V) during the 1980-85 period and two FSALs
during the late 1980s. A number of policy and institutional reforms were undertaken during this
period. The focus of both the FSALs was on financial policies, financial institutions and
regulations, and money and capital markets. The FSAL programs were, however, only partially
completed, as Turkey's political and economic arena remained caught up in ad hoc short term
choices during most of the 1990s, making i1 difficult to engage in long term policy and
institutional development decisions. Thus, actions on better banking regulations and supervision
and on bank privatization still remained on the agenda in the late 1990s.
107.  Assistance strategy. After some years of lower intensity interaction during the early and
mid 1990s, the level, nature and quality of the dialogue on the financial sector policy and
institutional issues between the Bank and the Government has recently substantially increased,
and has been quite high during the past three years. Elements of the Bank's strategy of lending
and non-lending assistance in the financial sector as the context for the proposed PFPSAL II are
as follows (see Figure 1 below).
108. Banking Reviews I and II. and FSAL - 1998-2000. Since early 1998 the Bank has been in
a substantive dialogue with the Government of Turkey regarding the health of its banking sector.
Right after the Russia crisis in August 1998, the Bank undertook a first Banking Review in
Turkey in November 1998, assessing the overall risks and vulnerabilities in the Turkish banking
system, and offering a set of recommendations for strengthening the legal and regulatory regime
and in carrying out structural reform in the banking sector. The newly elected Government of
Turkey during 1999 followed up on these recommendations through passage of a new Banking
Law, and specifically requested Bank support and advice for developing a full scale reform
program in the banking sector. In response, the Bank carried out a second Banking Review in
November 1999 outlining a reform program with specific recommendations and technical
advice. The finding of this review formed the basis for the design of the first Financial Sector
Adjustment Loan (FSAL) for US$778 million approved by the Board in December 2000. The
FSAL focused on (i) overhaul of the banking sector legal and regulatory regime to bring it more
in line with international standards, (ii) creation and operationalization of a new and more
independent Bank Regulation and Supervision Agency (BRSA) to take over bank regulation and
supervision responsibility from the Treasury and the Central Bank, (iii) strengthening the
Savings Deposit and Insurance Fund's (SDIF's) bank intervention and failure resolution
practices; and (iv) initiation of state bank restructuring and privatization. Both the banking
reviews, and the FSAL Board document clearly stated that there were serious vulnerabilities in
the banking system, and fairly high risks of a banking crisis. However, the FSAL got delayed
because the Government was unable to carry out the reforms agreed for nearly a year, and just
prior to the FSAL presentation to the Board, Turkey experienced its first banking crisis in
November/December 2000. This crisis confirmed the presence of the risks and vulnerabilities in
the banking system already pointed out earlier.

109.  Banking Review III, and PFPSAL I - 2001. Soon after this first crisis, and in the context
of rapidly deteriorating macro-economic conditions, when the second much more severe crisis
occurred in February 2001, the Bank undertook another Banking Review in early March 2001,
including comprehensive stress testing, to (i) identify the magnitude of already apparent and
possible future severe depletion of capital among Turkey's private banks; and (ii) outline the
urgent banking sector reform steps needed especially in the state banks and resolution of private
insolvent banks. To address the post crisis banking reform agenda highlighted in this review, and
the increasingly important macro-economic/public sector structural reform agenda, the Bank
prepared a single tranche Programmatic Financial and Public Sector Adjustment Loan
(PFPSAL-US$1.1 billion during the May-July 2001 period that was approved by the Board in
July 2001). The PFPSAL was planned as the first in a series of four programmatic financial
sector adjustment loans over a three year period, to address both the most urgent reform actions
and to deepen and sustain the necessary restructuring of the banking sector (the first two
programmatic adjustment loans have been planned as joint financial and public sector reform
loans). Specifically, the PFPSAL's conditionality in the banking area focused on accelerating
banking sector prudential regulation refonn-especially concerning loan loss provisioning and
connected lending, strengthening of the newly created BRSA through a targeted institutional
development effort, stepping up SDIF interventions in additional insolvent private banks and
actions to rapidly resolve these banks, and accelerating the financial and operational
restructuring of the state banks, including resolution of the duty loss issue (large paper claims on
the Government held by Ziraat and Halk, see para. 61), and closure of the insolvent Emlak Bank.
110. Critical PFPSAL Reform Actions Taken by the Government Since the February 2001
crisis. As part of the overall set of reform actions under the PFPSAL I, during April-November
2001, the Government in coordination with the CBT, the BRSA and the SDIF: (i) eliminated the
overnight short term repo liabilities in the SDIF banks and sharply reduced the overnight short
term repo liabilities in the state-owned banks Ziraat and Halk; (ii) satisfactorily capitalized the
SDIF banks and the state-owned banks with a view to reduce and eventually reverse their
continuing accumulation of losses; (iii) put in place a professional governance structure for the
state-owned banks Ziraat and Halk; (iv) revoked the banking license of Emlak, merged its
banking assets and liabilities with Ziraat and put the remaining shell into liquidation; (v)
aggressively pursued the closure/merger/sale of SDIF banks to complete their resolution as soon
as possible; (vi) intervened in another five insolvent private banks; and (vii) entered into capital
restoration plans with an additional 17 private banks with the objective to restore their capital
adequacy before the end of December 2001.
111.  PFPSAL II - 2002. The proposed PFPSAL II will further deepen the above reform
measures to restore the health of the banking sector through: (i) finalizing the overhaul of the
banking system prudential regime, while introducing targeted enforcement goals; (ii) completing
the implementation of the institutional development plan for the BRSA, and initiating a
comprehensive institutional development effort at the SDIF; (iii) completing the resolution of
banks intervened by the SDEF, and continuing the restructuring of undercapitalized and
otherwise non-compliant private sector banks; and (iv) completing the operational restructuring
of Ziraat and Halk; and the full privatization of Vakif, the remaining third state-owned bank.
Details are presented in section II above.



- 56 -
112.  NBFI Study, and PFSALs I and II - 2002-2003. The follow-up reform efforts in the
banking area (especially the completion of the state bank privatization effort) as well as the
reform efforts in the NBFI segment of the financial sector will be supported by two additional
programmatic financial sector adjustment loans during 2002 and 2003.
B.    The Bank's Public Sector Assistance Strategy
113.  The Bank's public sector assistance strategy is based on a carefully sequenced series of
lending operations backed by extensive economic and sector work and other non-lending
services. The first phase of the reform was supported by the Economic Reform Loan. It
involved fiscal measures to underpin the initial fiscal adjustment in 1999 and early 2000, and a
first round of budget reform measures including closure of two groups of budgetary and extra-
budgetary funds in December 1999 and March 2001. The ERL program also encompassed
implementation of the structural reform agenda for social security, energy, agriculture and
telecommunications which helps underpin the fiscal adjustment and contain the growth of quasi-
fiscal and contingent liabilities. The analytical basis for the ERL program was provided in large
part by the CEM on Structural Reforms for Sustainable Growth (September 2000) which
confirmed the public sector deficit as the core source of macroeconomic instability in Turkey and
detailed reforms in support of fiscal adjustment and growth.
114.  The second phase of the public sector reform was initiated by work on the PEIR which
presents an in-depth diagnosis of the operational and institutional problems confronting public
sector management and outlines a strategic framework for reform. The PEIR was complemented
by due diligence economic sector work in the form of the CPAR and CFAA which reviewed the
current legal framework and procedures for financial accountability in Turkey and developed
recommendations for refonn. This work provided much of the analytical framework for the first
PFPSAL approved in July. The first PFPSAL focused on launching a broader program of public
sector reforms, including a comprehensive public expenditure management reform, more
extensive structural fiscal reforms on both the revenue and expenditure sides, and the preparation
of a national anti-corruption strategy. The proposed PFPSAL II aims to deepen this agenda and
shift from strategy design to active implementation of reforms ranging from tax strategy and
public employment retrenchment to overhaul of the public procurement system and a national
program for fighting corruption. In addition to economic sector work, the Bank has supported
this effort through a series of international conferences designed to increase public awareness
and expose Turkey to international experience and best practice. The assistance strategy going
forward comprises a Programmable Public Sector Adjustment Loan (PPSAL) in 2003 to support
continued implementation of the reform agenda. A second PPSAL is tentatively envisaged
beyond the current CAS period. Elements of the Bank's strategy of lending and non-lending
assistance in the public sector as context for the proposed PFPSAL II are presented in Figure 2.

115.  Drawing a lesson from past experience, the current assistance strategy exploits the
advantages of the programmatic approach to support a comprehensive medium-term program to
modernize the public sector for which political support has been obtained in advance from the
highest levels of government. The current assistance program for public sector reform draws on
earlier efforts. In particular, the PFMP project approved in 1995 has achieved considerable
results in modernizing Turkey's customs service and improving the debt and cash management
functions in the Treasury, as well as design and development of the say2000i automated
accounting system. However, the PFMP was less successful in catalyzing improvements in tax
administration and public expenditure management. Political support for these objectives was
not adequately secured and bureaucratic inertia eventually set in. The tax and PEM measures
supported by the project were seen by many in the government as technical fixes of limited
significance in the absence of a broader reform vision for the public sector. The current
assistance strategy has learned from this experience. The emphasis in the early stages of the
program has been on the preparation of medium-term strategies underpinned by economic sector
work in the critical reform areas (including PEM, taxation, public employment, and anti-
corruption). The articulation of these strategies and the involvement of high-level steering
committees in their formulation and implementation has helped build both internal consensus as
well as the political and bureaucratic muscle needed to ensure effective implementation.
C.    PFPSAL II Objectives and Description
116.  The main objective of the proposed PFPSAL II is to support the next phase of the
Government's financial and public sector reform priorities in response to the February 2001
financial crisis, while continuing to ensure that social programs are adequately funded. Key
priorities include, in the financial sector: (i) overhaul of the regulatory framework for banking
activity, (ii) institutional development of the new Bank Regulation and Supervision Agency
(BRSA), (iii) problem bank/bank failure resolution, and (iv) state bank restructuring and
privatization; and in the public sector: (a) a further deepening of structural fiscal policies in
support of sustainable fiscal adjustment, (b) a broadening of the Government's public
expenditure management reform program including the implementation of concrete
improvements to the framework for budget planning and execution, financial accountability, and
public liability management, and (c) implementation of the Government's strategy to strengthen
public sector governance including adoption and implementation of a national anti-corruption
strategy and initial steps towards civil service reform. The proposed Second Programmatic
Financial and Public Sector Adjustment Loan (PFPSAL II) is the second Loan in support of the
Government's multi-year financial and public sector reform program. It follows on the first
Programmatic Financial and Public Sector Adjustment Loan of US$1.1 billion approved in July
2001 (US$700 million on standard IBRD terms and US$400 million on SSAL terms). As with
the first PFPSAL, the proposed PFPSAL II will provide budgetary support to help the
Government finance the costs arising from the crisis while continuing to fund its critical social
programs. The subsequent programmatic operations in the financial and public sectors planned
under the CAS will support a sustained effort by the Government to carry out this complex
reform agenda.



- 59 -
D.    Lessons Learned from PFPSAL I
117. The PFPSAL II operation has benefited from important lessons learned in the
implementation of PFPSAL I approved by the Board in July 2001. The first lesson is that the
approach of combining financial and public reforms into a single operation is well suited to the
Turkish context at this stage in time. The combination provides important synergies, including
in terms of ensuring consistency between fiscal adjustment and the financing requirements of the
bank restructuring program. The second lesson is that the urgency and complexity of the
financial and public sector reforms-juxtaposed with real world constraints on the institutional
capacity of the Turkish administration-argues for selectivity in determining core conditions and
flexibility with regards to the timetable for the implementation of specific reform measures. The
number of core conditions for the second and third tranches of PFPSAL II has been streamlined
in order to focus on the most important priorities while maintaining overall program coherence
as represented by the Letter of Development Policy and policy matrix. The multiple tranche
structure of the operation allows the Bank to stay closely engaged at this crucial point in the
reform program and provides additional flexibility to link the Bank's financial support more
effectively to the actual implementation of specific reform measures.
E.    Benefits and Risks
118.  Benefits. The principal benefits of the Loan will be to: (i) support the Government's
macroeconomic policy framework which aims to overcome the affects of the February crisis and
create the conditions for renewed growth and disinflation, (ii) restore confidence in the banking
system; (iii) strengthen the foundation for an efficient and sound banking system which can be
competitive in quality and performance at the international level; (iv) reduce the vulnerability of
the banking system and enhance its capacity to withstand external shocks and thereby reduce
systemic failure risk; (v) position Turkey's banking sector for accession to the European Union
(EU) by starting the process of aligning the prudential regime for the banking system with
applicable EU banking sector directives; (vi) support structural fiscal measures to underpin the
sustainability and quality of the fiscal adjustment needed to achieve macroeconomic stability
while ensuring that social spending is protected; (vii) lay the foundation for durable
improvements to public expenditure management and fiscal transparency in line with
international standards; and (viii) raise the quality of public sector governance. The PFPSAL II
will provide essential budgetary support to help the Government finance the costs of recovering
from the crisis while continuing to fund its critical social programs.
119.  Risks and Mitigating Measures. Turkey's financial and public sector reform program
entails significant risks, but these continue to be outweighed by the benefits of the reforms and
the risks of not proceeding. The situation in Turkey will be subject to continued uncertainty as
events unfold on the ground. The three main risk factors are:
* the difficult macroeconomic conditions arising from the crisis,
* a potential loss of consensus for political or social reasons, and
* institutional challenges in implementing the program.



- 60 -
120.  The active support of the Bank is instrumental in helping the Government overcome
these risks and sustain implementation of its reform program. The Bank's assistance strategy for
financial and public sector reform incorporates significant mitigation measures. However, the
risks cannot be fully offset given the severity of the crisis and extent of the structural and
institutional reforms required. In response to the situation, the PFPSAL, originally envisaged as
a single tranche operation, has been restructured into three tranches which will disburse in
sequence with the concrete implementation of key measures under the program and the
progressive establishment of a track record of satisfactory macroeconomic outcomes over the
first half of 2002
121.  Macroeconomic risks.   The shocks generated by the February crisis continue to
reverberate in the Turkish economy and it has taken longer than initially expected to restore
stability to the financial markets. As mentioned earlier, the economy was hit by a new external
shock following the September 11 terrorist attack in the United States. With the recent
improvement in financial indicators, the balance of macroeconomic risk is shifting to possible
delays in economic recovery. Resumption of growth is critical to ensuring both technical
requirements such as debt sustainability as well as broader social support for the reform program.
Economic activity has slowed sharply since the crisis and the recession in 2001 has been much
deeper and more prolonged than expected. The actual pace of recovery will depend heavily on
the Government's ability to sustain an aggressive pace of reform.
122.  A key concern for growth continues to be that actual ex post real interest rates will
exceed program levels which would slow the recovery, negatively affect public debt
sustainability and create the conditions for renewed instability. Real interest rates have declined

The Government's market friendly reform strategy and the exceptional external financing from
the IFIs are designed to bolster credibility and rebuild confidence. Confidence will also be
helped by the fact that the Government is now implementing a detailed medium-term program
for addressing structural weaknesses which have built up over many years. Nevertheless, the
fragility of Turkey's banking sector and public finances will take time to correct, and the country
will remain vulnerable to internal and external shocks over the medium term.

123.    Public debt sustainability.       Sustainability of the public debt-which         is critical to
macroeconomic stability-will hinge on strong macroeconomic policies and accelerated
structural reform to build confidence. Under the program, the public debt to GNP ratio (net of
CBT net assets) is projected to fall from 91 percent in 2001 to 81.3 percent in 2002, a drop of
some 10 percentage points (Table 13). The debt burden is projected to decline further in 2003-
04 on the assumption that interest rates will continue to fall and the recovery will be sustained.
Realizing this outcome will depend on rapid economic recovery and falling interest rates, but it
also depends on other crucial factors such as the maturity of new borrowing and developments in
the non-central government components of the public sector. The program scenario assumes that
the maturity in 2002 of new borrowing by the Treasury in TL in the domestic auction market
stays at just under six months while improving to around 7.5 months in 2003, and that Treasury
will undertake a significant amount of new         domestic borrowing (about 15 percent of total
domestic borrowing) in FX-denominated/FX-linked instruments at maturities of one year or
more.26 With regard to the non-central govermnent components of the public sector, projected
26 The program baseline also assumes that the state banks will receive TL 8.3 quadrillion in interest due on
recapitalization bonds in 2002 based on a restructuring of these securities carried out in December 2001 and January
2002.



- 62 -
improvements in the financial position of the rest of the public sector, outside of the central
government and CBT, account for about four percentage points (i.e., about one-third) of the
improvement in the public debt to GNP ratio in 2002 under the program. This adjustment will
require an acceleration and deepening of structural reform and retrenchment in the broader
public sector, particularly the SEEs, beyond what Turkey has been able to achieve so far.
124.  A sensitivity analysis of the public debt dynamics demonstrates the central role of
credible policies to build confidence and deepen the fiscal adjustment. A downside scenario has
been prepared in which: (i) the decline in interest rates lags the program path by one quarter, (ii)
there is no improvement in the average maturity of new domestic borrowing and no new FX-
linked borrowing, and (iii) the public sector outside of the central government and CBT is unable
to improve its financial position. Under these assumptions growth is projected to reach only one
percent in 2002 and three percent in 2003. All other basic macroeconomic parameters remain
unchanged from the program baseline. Under the downside scenario, the public debt to GNP
ratio declines over the medium term, but at a much slower pace than in the program baseline and
domestic debt amortization remains high as a share of GNP. This is a clear indication that
domestic financial market conditions must continue to improve substantially-in parallel with
permanent fiscal adjustment in the broader public sector--in order for debt sustainability to be
ensured. This requires further gains in investor confidence which in turn will depend on the
credibility of the Government's program and the degree of support for the program from the
international community.
125.  External vulnerability. Turkey's external vulnerability is exemplified by the September
11 shock and the large capital outflows registered in 2001. A further indicator are the gross
financing requirements of some US$20 billion per year projected over 2002-04 (Table 14) which
now represent a greater proportion of GNP after the devaluation. Given the capital outflows, the
stock of external debt is now projected to stabilize in 2001 at the level of US$117 billion (with
the short-term debt stock falling sharply to US$18 billion). However, as a result of the
devaluation, this will equal 78 percent of GNP, a sharp increase compared to 59 percent in 2000.
Strong export performance kept the debt service ratio to under 45 percent in 2001. While
expected to improve under the program, the debt service ratio is projected to remain above 40
percent through 2003. The high level of capital outflows estimated for 2001 indicates that efforts
to encourage private sector involvement (PSI), i.e., "bail in" by private creditors, had limited
impact in 2001. Looking ahead, the extent to which Turkish banks and enterprises will be able
to rollover their external obligations will remain an important determinant for the program.
Slowing the capital outflow in 2002, and then reversing this trend in 2003-04, will be
instrumental in relieving pressure on the exchange rate and creating the conditions for a
sustained decline in domestic interest rates.
126.  Exceptional financing from the IFIs is providing Turkey with needed support at this
critical juncture, but a return of investor confidence will be essential to ultimate success of the
program. At the same time, the country is faced with an important opportunity. Most of the
capital outflow witnessed in 2001 and projected for 2002 is in the form of short-term debt and
portfolio investment. As private capital returns to Turkey over the medium term, the extent to
which a significant share of the "hot money" outflow can be replaced with longer maturity
inflows-especially FDI, the country's external vulnerability will be permanently reduced. In



- 63 -
this regard, decisive implementation of the action plan to promote FDI recently announced by
the Government will be extremely important.

127.  Political and social risks. The financial and public sector reforms pose major political
challenges to the Government and there are certain social risks as well. The resilience of the
coalition has been tested since February 2001. Political debates on the program among the
coalition parties, which surfaced strongly during the Summer, have now become less intense and
less frequent. The ability of the coalition to survive depends upon continued timely delivery of
the reform program and awareness of this is keeping the Government focused on the task at
hand. At the same time, sustained implementation of the reforms will test the coalition further in
2002. The commercialization and privatization of the state-owned banks is a major political
hurdle for the Government. The restructuring and privatization of Halk and Ziraat will have
social implications as their workforces are downsized, although this is mitigated to a significant
extent by the relatively extensive social protection programs for civil servants and public sector
workers. The reform agenda for the public sector is also politically sensitive. Institutional
change can face resistance from vested interest groups that would prefer to maintain the status
quo. To mitigate this risk, the program relies on strong consensus and up-front ownership of the
strategy by the Government and in particular key ministries. Sustained fiscal adjustment is
generating social costs and incomes policy and action to retrench staffing levels in the public
sector is particularly sensitive. The Government is trying to mitigate these risks by strengthening
its social protection programs and through dialogue with key social partners. The social risks of
the broader economic reform program are substantial and cannot be fully mitigated despite
efforts to protect social spending and improve targeted social assistance. The Government has
taken steps to strengthen its social protection programs under the SRMP project with the Bank,
including the introduction of conditional cash transfers targeted to families with many children.
The introduction of direct income support starting in late 2001 will ease the burden of the crisis
on farmers. To help build and sustain social consensus for the program during the tough
austerity phase, the Government has moved to strengthen its public information and outreach
efforts.
128. Implementation risks. The complex reforms which make up the program are challenging
the institutional capacity of the administration. In the financial sector, the BRSA has made
impressive strides as explained in Part II, but must continue to build up its implementation
capacity. The program includes support to the BRSA and SDIF, but their learning curves will
continue to be steep and tested by the crisis. To some extent, crisis management efforts at key
Government agencies such as the MOF, SPO and Treasury have deflected attention from slower
moving public sector reforms, and this explains in part the delays in meeting some of the
benchmarks for PFPSAL II. Looking ahead, institutional capacity constraints are likely to be a
serious obstacle to public sector reform implementation at all levels of government. The need
for extensive institution building and training to progress with public sector reform is one of the
major rationales for the programmatic approach being followed by the Bank. The Bank has
continued to provide intensive technical support for key public sector reform measures including
technical assistance during the second half of 2001 for the development of the tax strategy and
for the Public Investment Review. Further technical support will be provided in 2002 with an
increased focus on public liability management and implementation of the new public
procurement law. The Bank stands ready to consider financial support for selected institution-
building initiatives linked to the program.



- 65 -
129.  Regulatory forbearance. Satisfactory and timely failure resolution efforts by the SDIF
(for already intervened banks) and enforcement of capital restoration plans by the new Bank
Regulation and Supervision Agency for non-intervened banks are important. There is a risk that
such efforts will fall short of what is needed due to political pressure that may be put on these
agencies for a slow-down of regulatory enforcement action and general regulatory forbearance.
To mitigate these risks, the proposed program has specifically focused on strengthening both the
monitoring and enforcement functions of the BRSA. While the monitoring of capital adequacy
and foreign exchange exposure of banks has been significantly strengthened, efforts are being
made to focus on large/connected exposures, and close monitoring and corrective actions will be
planned and closely monitored to reduce risks due to such exposures. With regards to capital
adequacy levels of private banks, BRSA is insisting on full scale portfolio audits to correctly
reflect the correct capital levels. This will be followed by a public solvency support scheme
implemented by BRSA. With these actions, the total systemic risk due to low capital levels will
be considerably mitigated.
F.    Conditions for Board Presentation and Release of the Second and Third Tranches
130.  The PFPSAL II is a three tranche loan. The actions listed below are conditions for Board
presentation, second tranche release and third tranche release as noted. For the second and third
tranches of PFPSAL II, these core conditions are included in the legal agreement. The timing of
tranche release will depend on the actual fulfillment of the agreed core conditions. For further
details see the policy matrix (Annex VI).
I.    Macroeconomic Environment
Board Presentation
* Agreement on a satisfactory macro framework for 2002 consistent with the core objectives
including GNP growth of 3 percent, annual inflation of 35 percent and nominal interest rates
in the 55 petcent range by end-2002, a primary surplus for the consolidated public sector of
6.5 percent of GNP, and a decline in the public debt to GNP ratio to about 81 percent.
*  Satisfactory budget for 2002 is approved by Parliament which ensures satisfactory social
expenditure envelopes for health, education and social protection.
Second Tranche
* Satisfactory macroeconomic program including track record for at least 2002(Q1) to be
evaluated on the basis of the overall program and with respect to domestic interest rates,
inflation, maturity of new government domestic borrowing, budget outcome, and private
capital inflows.
Third Tranche
* Satisfactory macroeconomic program including track record for at least 2002(Q2) to be
evaluated on the basis of the overall program and with respect to domestic interest rates,
inflation, maturity of new government domestic borrowing, budget outcome, and private
capital inflows.



- 66 -
II.   Bankine Reform
A.    Regulatory Framework for Banking Activity:
Board Presentation
 The BRSA to (i) issue an explanatory supplement to the LLP rule to clarify the concept of
fair value for the valuation of collateral supporting bank loans; (ii) to amend the provisional
article 2 (f) in the Banking Law 4389 so as to eliminate the phasing-in of specific loan loss
provisioning requirements for classified exposures over a period of four years starting June
1999; and (iii) to amend the LLP rule to facilitate restructuring of corporate loans; and ensure
restructured loans are only upgraded to a higher classification category once renewed debt
service track record and creditworthiness are firmly established.
* The BRSA to require banks to bring repos on balance sheet as collateralized finance
transactions.
Second Tranche
* Develop detailed actions plans for banks with excess large/connected exposures to reach
compliance with the new banking law limits. Government/BRSA to address any remaining
legal/tax hurdles to merger/de-mergers necessary for implementing such action plans.
Third Tranche
* Satisfactory implementation of the action plans for banks with excess large/connected
exposures.
B.    Institutional Development of the BRSA/SDIF:
Second Tranche
* The SDIF staff to prepare, and the SDIF Board to approve a time-bound strategic plan, with
clearly assigned implementation responsibilities that (i) puts in place a more effective
organizational structure, with clearly separated line responsibilities for bank resolution effort,
asset management efforts, administration of the blanket guarantee & the deposit insurance
scheme and supporting functions such as legal, human resources and information technology;
(ii) sets performance targets for the bank resolution and asset management functions; (iii)
envisages the hiring of additional qualified staff to handle the high volume of non-
performing loans and other problem assets to be transferred to the Collection Department for
collection/workout; and (iv) builds a centralized and high quality management information
system that will allow SDIF senior management and the SDIF Board to monitor progress
being made towards reaching agreed resolution and asset management/collection targets.
C.    Problem Bank/Bank Failure Resolution:
Board Presentation
* The BRSA to undertake the necessary regulatory and enforcement actions including hiring of
external auditors to launch the process of ensuring that all non performing loans are correctly
and transparently stated through proper classification and provisioning procedures in the
private deposit taking banks' balance sheets in order to reflect the true capital position of the
banking system.



- 67 -
*  Resolve Eti, Iktisat, Site, Kent, Bayindir, and EGS through sale or license revocation/
liquidation. (one of the banks may be converted into a non deposit bank for residual asset
resolution purposes.
*  Resolve all the liabilities remaining with the SDIF upon completion of the sales of some of
the SDIF banks or revocation of the banking licenses of unsaleable banks, by transferring all
such banking liabilities along with matching Government securities to large private banks
(including Ziraat) through auctions. SDIF to also prepare an asset resolution strategy to deal
with all the remaining non financial assets, non performing loans, and other investments in
subsidiaries of these banks.
Second Tranche
*  The BRSA to issue satisfactory implementing regulations for the public support scheme for
re-capitalization of private deposit taking banks.
*  SDIF to carry out satisfactory implementation of the asset resolution strategy including
transfer of all remaining assets to a specific asset resolution entity or its own departments.
*  The Government to undertake a co-coordinated review of the improvements required in the
legal framework for bankruptcy and collateral foreclosure and accelerated/more efficient
NPL resolution.
Third Tranche
*  Satisfactory implementation of capital restoration plans with capital deficient banks, or
implementation of satisfactory SDIF resolution actions including interventions, mergers/
closures for such banks.
*  Complete the resolution of any banks taken over by the SDIF in late 2001, as well as Turk
Ticaret and Taris banks, subject to the final Administrative Court decisions allowing their
resolution.
D.    State Bank Restructuring and Privatization:
Board Presentation
* The Government to issue instructions to the Ziraat and Halk management to undertake the
necessary operational restructuring including unprofitable branch closures and reduction of
excess staff.
*  Agreement to be reached between the Treasury and the Joint State Banks Management on the
amount of interest and principal payments to be made in cash during 2002 on Government
securities in the books of Ziraat and Halk.
*  Identify all public service functions of Ziraat and Halk, that should be transferred to other
entities as well as the recipient entities for these functions and formally adopt a transfer plan
through issuance of Government of Turkey decision/decree/legislation/protocols as
necessary.
* Hire a Privatization Advisor to advise on the Vakif privatization strategy.
Second Tranche
*  Satisfactory substantial progress to be made towards unprofitable branch closures and
reduction of excess staff by Ziraat and Halk.
* All interest/principal payments to Ziraat and Halk on Government securities necessary for
elimination of short term liabilities on the books of Ziraat and Halk during 2002 to be made
in cash as per the agreement reached with the Treasury.



- 68 -
Third Tranche
*  All necessary branch closures and reduction of excess staff to be completed.
* All interest/principal payments on Government Bonds necessary for elimination of short term
liabilities on the books of Ziraat and Halk to continue to be made in cash during 2002 as per
the agreement reached with the Treasury and adjusted for any major changes in the liability
maturities in Ziraat and Halk.
*  Complete the privatization (100 percent) of Vakif.
III.  Public Sector Reform
A.    Structural Fiscal Policies:
Board Presentation

> Revenue increases including increase in petroleum consumption tax, special transaction
taxes, property tax revenues, and real increases in SEE prices in late 2001 and
adjustments in line with the WPI or world prices in 2002.
> Reduction in personnel and benefit expenditures including retrenchment of SEE
employment and changes in health insurance benefits.
> Reduction in operating expenditures including elimination of regional administrations,
shift of certain departments to the provincial administration, and tighter restrictions on
SEE expenditures.
> Further reduction in indirect agriculture subsidies.
* Medium-term strategy to improve the tax system is adopted by the Government.
* Comprehensive public employment program to adjust public sector staffing levels is
initiated.
Second Tranche
* Implementation of medium-term strategy to improve the tax system is satisfactory.
* Implementation of comprehensive public employment program is satisfactory.
Third Tranche
* Implementation of medium-term strategy to improve the tax system is satisfactory.
* Implementation of comprehensive public employment program is satisfactory.

G.    Triggers for the PFPSAL II, and Follow-up PFSAL I & II and PPSAL I & II; and
Program Outcome Indicators
131.  In view of the fact that the planned financial and public sector reform program is being
supported by the Bank through a series of Programmatic Adjustment Loans, there is a certain
flexibility in the final conditionally for each loan based on actual developments in the relevant
sectors. However, while retaining this flexibility, there are some critical necessary conditions
(program triggers) to be implemented in any event to ensure that the reform program is
implemented in line with the agreed program. These necessary conditions or triggers for each of
the programmatic loans are presented in Table 15 below along with the reform outcome
indicators for the program as a whole. The triggers will be refined as the program moves
forward and the set of specific outcome indicators will be expanded in collaboration with the
Government. As explained above, the Government has met most of the triggers for PFPSAL II
as presented in the Memorandum of the President for the first PFPSAL in July 2001, and will
meet all the remaining triggers prior to release of the third tranche of the loan.
H.    Financial Management
132.  The Undersecretariat of Treasury has in place adequate accounting and financial
management reporting systems for the two Deposit Accounts (one each for the standard IBRD
and SSAL components under the loan). The Treasury will maintain records of all transactions
under the loan in accordance with sound accounting practices. The Government will submit to
the Bank a monthly receipts and payments account showing the transactions on the Deposit
Accounts, starting with the receipt of the first tranche of Bank funds and ending when the final
tranche of loan funds has been received and the balance on the Deposit Accounts has been
reduced to nil. The Borrower shall: (a) have the Deposit Accounts audited in accordance with
appropriate auditing principles consistently applied by independent auditors acceptable to the
Bank; (b) furnish to the Bank as soon as available but in any case not later than four months after
the date of the Bank's request for such audit, a certified copy of the report of such audit by said
auditors of such scope and in such detail as the Bank shall have reasonable required; and (c)
furnish to the Bank such other information concerning the Deposit Accounts and the audit
thereof as the Bank shall have reasonably requested. The audit will be carried out at quarterly
intervals as agreed with the Bank, calculated from the date the first tranche of loan funds is paid
into the Deposit Accounts, with suitable adjustments of timing depending on the funds left in the
Deposit Accounts at any point in time. The terms of reference for the audit will be agreed
between the Bank and the Borrower. Audits would end once the final tranche of loan funds has
been received and all funds have been transferred from the Deposit Accounts. In addition to the
above, the Bank may request that its own staff (or a specialist contractor working on its behalf)
carry out special reviews of the Deposit Accounts. If, after deposit into the Deposit Accounts,
the proceeds of this loan are used for ineligible purposes, the Bank will require the Borrower to
either: (i) return that amount to the account for use for eligible purposes; or (ii) refund the
amount directly to the Bank.
